Ashʿarī Answer to the First Objection on the Qualifier
Fragment of Ashʿarī reply that opponent also uses qualifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarī respond to the objection that their demand for a qualifier is arbitrary, by showing that the opponent also uses qualifiers like 'color and colored'.

Core Idea
The qualifier is not unique to Ashʿarī theology — opponents use it too.

The Ashʿarī are often challenged for requiring a 'qualifier' (musahhih) for vision of Allah. In this passage, they turn the tables and argue that their opponents themselves rely on qualifiers, such as 'color and colored' for vision of accidents. This shows the demand is a shared rational requirement, not an Ashʿarī invention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text is incomplete but the main argument is clear.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the key point is present.
2
Identify the argument
The Ashʿarī say the demand for a qualifier is not specific to them. Why? Because the opponents use color and colored as a qualifier.
This is a rebuttal: the opponent’s own practice includes a qualifier.
3
Reflect on the implication
If both sides use qualifiers, then the Ashʿarī position is not arbitrary. This undermines the objection.
This is a common theological strategy: showing consistency with opponent’s methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : الجواب عن الاعتراض الأول : أن طلب المصحح لم يختص بنا، فإنكم جعلتم اللون والمتلون مصححا ، وذلك أن الرؤية لما تعلقت ببعض الموجودات وذلك نوعان؛ نوع من الأعراض ونوع من الجواهر، فصح كون ...
The Ashʿariyya said: The answer to the first objection is that the demand for a qualifier (musahhih) is not specific to us. For you have made color and the colored a qualifier. That is because vision attaches to some existents, and they are of two types: a type of accidents and a type of substances. So it is correct that...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the Ashʿarī response to the first objection (which is not included in this window, but presumably objected to the Ashʿarī use of existence as qualifier). The Ashʿarī argue that the demand for a qualifier is not unique to their position because the opponents themselves also use qualifiers: they make color and the colored (al-mutalawwin) as qualifiers for vision. The reasoning is that vision attaches to some existents, which are divided into two types: accidents and substances. The argument seems to be that the opponents must also specify a qualifier (such as color for accidents), so the Ashʿarī are not alone in needing a qualifier. The passage is incomplete, cutting off at 'fasaḥḥa kawn...' (so it is correct that...), but the main point is established: the demand for a qualifier is shared by both sides.

المتلون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ashʿarī arbitrarily demand a qualifier for vision of Allah.
Correction: The Ashʿarī argue that the opponent also uses a qualifier (color and colored), so the demand is not arbitrary but a shared rational requirement.

Checkpoint
What is the Ashʿarī’s main point in their answer to the first objection?
A) The qualifier for vision is only existence.
B) The opponent also uses a qualifier (color and colored) for vision.
C) The demand for a qualifier is unique to the Ashʿarī.
D) Vision does not need any qualifier.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī rebut the objection by showing that the demand for a qualifier is not unique to them, as opponents also use qualifiers like color and colored.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'ari point out that their opponents unknowingly rely on the same method they criticize. Is there an area in your own life where you expect consistency from others but fail to apply the same standard to yourself? What would it take for you to align your actions with your convictions?
?
When you defend your beliefs, do you ever find yourself using arguments that, if applied consistently, would undermine those very beliefs? Take a moment to examine one of your firmly held opinions—could it depend on a 'qualifier' you have not acknowledged?
?
The passage shows that both sides need a qualifier—some condition that makes perception possible. Do you ever treat created things (like good health, knowledge, or social approval) as necessary conditions for spiritual growth, forgetting that God is not bound by such qualifiers? How might you adjust your trust to rest only on God's unlimited power?

Existence as the Common Ground for Vision
Positive argument establishing existence as the common cause for vision's attachment to substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existence serves as the common cause that makes both substances and accidents eligible for vision in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Existence is the single intellectual meaning that unites vision's attachment to substance and accident.

This passage presents a key argument in rational theology: what makes something visible? The author argues that existence (wujud) is the common factor that grounds the possibility of seeing both substances and accidents. This reasoning follows the established method of analogical inference from the visible world to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense logical structure: claim, definition, inference, conclusion, analogy.
2
Identify the claim and definition
The author states that vision relates to two genera as a single case. He then defines 'validity' (sihha) as the eligibility of each genus for vision. Understand these two points as the starting premise.
3
Understand the need for a uniform cause
Since the validity is uniform, the cause necessitating that validity must also be uniform. Ask yourself: what could be common to both substances and accidents that makes them visible?
This is the logical bridge to the next step.
4
Existence as the common meaning
The author proposes existence (wujud) as that uniform cause. Even though existence differs in reality for different things, in the intellect it is a single concept covering both. This is the core insight.
Reflect on how existence is abstracted as a singular meaning.
5
Connect to the analogy method
The author compares this reasoning to the established method of analogical inference from the seen (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) using paired concepts. See how the pairs reinforce the logic.
This shows the argument is grounded in a standard kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما قطعنا بأن الرؤية تعلقت بجنسي قضية واحدة، وإنما نعني بالصحة صلاحية كل جنس لتعلق الرؤية، فتلك الصلاحية صحتها على نعت واحد وإنما العلة المقتضية لتلك الصلاحية يجب أن تكون على نعت واحد، والوجود وإن اختلف بالنسبة إلى المختلفات غير أنه في العقل والتصور معنى واحد يشمل القسمين، فصح أن يكون مناطًا للحكم، وهذا كما قررناه في الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب بالشرط والمشروط، والعلة والمعلول، والدليل والمدلول، والحقيقة والمحقق.
And when we have firmly established that vision is related to two genera (substances and accidents) as a single case, and by "validity" we mean the eligibility of each genus for the attachment of vision, then that validity has its validity in a single mode, and the efficient cause necessitating that validity must be in a single mode. And existence, even though it differs in relation to different things, is in the intellect and conception a single meaning that includes both divisions, so it is valid to be the locus of the judgment. And this is as we have established in the analogy between the visible (shahid) and the absent (gha'ib) by way of condition and conditioned, cause and effect, proof and proved, and reality and realized.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents a positive argument for the common cause (illa) that makes vision applicable to both substances (jawhar) and accidents (arad). The author begins by asserting that vision attaches to two genera as a single case. By "validity" (sihha) he means the eligibility (salahiyya) of each genus to be seen. This validity must be uniform in mode, and the cause that necessitates it must also be uniform. Existence (wujud) is then proposed as the common factor: although existence differs in relation to different things (i.e., substance exists differently than accident), in the intellect and conception it is a single meaning covering both. Therefore, existence can serve as the basis (manat) for the judgment that both are visible. The author likens this reasoning to the method of analogy between the visible world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib), using paired concepts: condition and conditioned, cause and effect, proof and proved, reality and realized. The lesson is a clear application of the qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid method, a cornerstone of kalam reasoning.

شاهد وغائب
شرط ومشروط
علة ومعلول
دليل ومدلول
حقيقة ومحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'validity' (sihha) refers to the actual occurrence of vision rather than the ontological eligibility to be seen.
Correction: In this passage, 'validity' specifically means the eligibility or suitability of a thing to be an object of vision, not the actual act of seeing.

Checkpoint
What common meaning does the author identify as the basis for vision's attachment to both substance and accident?
A) Color
B) Shape
C) Existence
D) Materiality

Key Takeaway
Existence is the uniform cause that makes both substances and accidents eligible for vision in the intellect, established through analogical reasoning from the visible world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is the single common meaning that makes both physical and non-physical things eligible for vision, does your heart truly perceive Allah's existence as more real than the objects around you? Or do you treat His existence as a distant concept? What would change in your daily life if you 'saw' His existence as directly as you see the existence of a chair?
?
The author insists the cause must be uniform. In your own spiritual life, do you seek a single, deep cause for your connection to Allah, or do you scatter your attention among many superficial reasons? How can you focus on the one essential cause—His existence—to make your worship more consistent?
?
When you reflect on the analogy of shahid and gha'ib, are you willing to apply the same logical consistency to your faith as you do to everyday reasoning? What specific aspect of your belief in the unseen (like the attributes of Allah) have you been treating as less certain than visible things? How will you align your conviction with the proof given here?

How Vision Relates to Substance and Accident
Answer to objection on vision and substance/accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a common rectifier is necessary for vision to perceive both substance and accident, and why color or the colored object cannot be that cause.

Core Idea
Vision perceives shape and color together, so a single cause must underlie both.

This passage answers a key objection about how Allah's vision (ru'ya) can attach to both substance and accident. The author uses everyday experience to show that we perceive volume and shape (substance) independently from color (accident), yet both are seen simultaneously. This points to a common rectifier that makes both visible, and rules out color or the colored thing as the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the empirical example about seeing a person from a distance.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Find where the author describes how we perceive volume, shape, and color. Note that shape is perceived first, then color, yet both are perceived by sight.
The observation leads to the need for a common rectifier.
3
Understand the refutation
The author rejects the opponent's view that color or the colored thing is the rectifier. Count the two reasons given: composition and diversification.
This refutation strengthens the author's own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الاعتراض الثالث وهو إجمال القول في تعلق الرؤية بالجوهر والعرض، قلنا: الإنسان يدرك من نفسه إدراك الحجمية الجوهر وتحيزه وشكله من تدوير وتثليث وتربيع وتخميس، إلى غير ذلك من الإشكال، ولهذا يبصر شخصا من بُعد فيدري شخصيته ويشك في لونه وصغره وكبره كما ذكرنا أسباب ذلك، ثم يدرك بعد شكله ولونه فعرف أن الإشكال والألوان غير والجسم من حيث هر جسم غير في البصر، وقد أدركهما البصر جميعا، فلا بد من مصحح جامع بينهما وذلك ما عيناه، ولذلك قلنا : إن اللون والمتلون لن يصلح أن يكون علة ؛ لأنه تركيب في العلة، ومع كونه تركيبا هو تشكيل، فإنهم يقولون : المصحح هو اللون أو المتلون، فلا العلة المركبة صالحة ولا التشكيل في العلة مستعمل، فبطل ما نصبوه علة وصح ما نصبناه.
As for the answer to the third objection, which is a summary of the statement concerning the attachment of vision to substance and accident, we say: Man perceives from himself the perception of the volume of substance, its occupancy of space, and its shape—circular, triangular, square, pentagonal—and other shapes. For this reason, he sees a person from a distance, recognizes his personality, but doubts his color and size—small or large—as we have mentioned the causes of that. Then he perceives afterward his shape and color. Thus he knows that shapes and colors are different, and that the body as a body is not something in vision? And vision has perceived both of them (shape and color) together, so there must be a common rectifier between them, and that is what we have specified. Therefore we said: Color and the colored thing cannot be a cause, because it would be a composition in the cause, and in addition to being a composition, it is a diversification. For they say the rectifier is color or the colored thing. Thus neither the composite cause is valid nor is diversification used in the cause. So what they set up as a cause is invalid, and what we set up is valid.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is the answer to a specific objection about how vision (ru'ya) can relate to both substance (jawhar) and accident (arad). The author uses empirical observation: a person perceives the volume, spatial occupancy, and shape of a substance—such as circular, triangular, etc. When seeing a person from afar, one first recognizes the person's identity but is uncertain about color and size; later, shape and color are perceived. This shows that shape and color are distinct, and the body as a body is not itself in the vision? The key point: the sense of sight perceives both shape and color together, so there must be a common rectifier (musahhih) that makes both perceivable. The author then refutes the alternative that color or the colored thing (mutalawin) is the cause. If color were the cause, it would involve composition (tarkib) in the cause, and moreover it would be a diversification (tashkil) because color is particular. The opponents' claim that the rectifier is color or the colored thing is invalid because a composite cause is not suitable, and diversification is not used in causation. Therefore, their proposed cause fails, and the author's own explanation (existence as the common factor) is validated.

عرض
حجمية
شكل
لون
متلون
مصحح
تشكيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Color or the colored object is the cause for vision's attachment to visible things.
Correction: The passage argues that if that were the case, the cause would involve composition and diversification, which is invalid. Instead, a common rectifier (existence) is needed.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject color or the colored thing as the cause of vision's attachment?
A) Because color is not real
B) Because it would involve composition and diversification
C) Because color is an accident
D) Because color is not perceived at a distance

Key Takeaway
The perception of both substance and accident by vision requires a common rectifier, not a composite cause like color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses your own experience of seeing a person from a distance to reason about causation. When was the last time you used a personal experience to reflect on the reality of God? What held you back from doing so more often?
?
The opponents assumed color or the colored object was the cause of vision. What assumptions do you hold about your own abilities or the world that might crumble under scrutiny? Are you willing to test them against revelation and reason?
?
This argument leads to the conclusion that a single common rectifier must exist for both substance and accident. If you accept that, what then is the implication for how you view your own existence and your relationship with your Creator? Will you let this lesson change how you see the world today?

Why Vision Does Not Attach to All Accidents
Reply to objection that vision should attach to all accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response to the objection that vision should attach to all accidents, and why existence is the universal cause for visibility.

Core Idea
Vision's limited scope leads to finding a universal cause—existence.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical objection: if vision has a common cause, why does it only attach to some accidents? He uses an analogy with knowledge to show that limited attachment prompts the search for a cause. Then he demonstrates that the common factor cannot be color alone, because vision attaches to many accidents beyond color, forcing a generalization to existence as the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Begin by reading the first part of the passage where the objection is stated and the author responds with a conditional. Notice how the author says 'If it were related to all accidents, we would not need to seek a cause.'
This sets up the logic that limited attachment requires causal explanation.
2
Follow the analogy with knowledge
The author draws an analogy with knowledge: knowledge attaches to all knowable things, so no cause is sought. Vision attaches only to some, so a cause must exist. Understand how this analogy supports the necessity of searching for a cause.
3
Identify the accidents vision attaches to
Locate the list of accidents mentioned: color, conjunction, separation, contact, and alignment. Note that these are diverse and not reducible to color alone.
This list is key to rejecting the opponent's proposed cause (color).
4
Understand the search for a common factor
See how the author sought a common factor between substances and these accidents but found none in 'color' or 'colored thing'. The failure forces a broader generalization.
The common factor must apply to both substances and accidents.
5
Grasp the conclusion: existence as universal cause
The passage concludes that the judgment must be generalized to every accident due to the common factor between the two genera (substance and accident), which is existence. This is the universal cause of visibility.
This answers the original objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : لم تتعلق الرؤية بجميع الأعراض). قلنا : لولو تعلقت حسنًا بجميع الأعراض ما كنا نحتاج إلى طلب العلة، كما لم نحتج إلى طلب العلة لتعلق العلم بالمعلوم، إذ تعلق بكل ما يصح أن يعلم معدوماً أو موجودا محالاً أو ممكنا ، لكنا نطلب العلة ؛ لأنها تعلقت بالبعض، وكما تعلقت ببعض الأعراض تعلقت بجميع الأجرام، فطلبنا جامعا ولم نظفر بجامع بين الجواهر وبعض الأعراض، كما صاروا إليه من اللون والمتلون، فإن الرؤية تعلقت باللون كما تعلقت بالاجتماع والافتراق والمماسة والمحاذاة ، وهي أعراض وراء اللون، فلم يكن بد من تعميم الحكم في كل عرض لعموم الجامع بين الجنسين،
And their statement: 'Why is vision not related to all accidents?' We say: If it were related well to all accidents, we would not need to seek a cause, just as we did not need to seek a cause for the attachment of knowledge to the known, since it attaches to everything that can be known—non-existent or existent, impossible or possible. But we seek a cause because it is related to some (accidents). And just as it is related to some accidents, it is related to all bodies. So we sought a common factor and did not find a common factor between substances and some accidents, such as what they held to be color and the colored thing. For vision is related to color as it is related to conjunction, separation, contact, and alignment, which are accidents beyond color. So there was no alternative but to generalize the judgment to every accident due to the generality of the common factor between the two genera.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage answers the objection: 'Why doesn't vision attach to all accidents?' The author responds by analogy with knowledge: if knowledge attached to everything, we would not seek a cause for its attachment; but vision attaches only to some accidents, so we seek a cause. Vision attaches to some accidents (like color, conjunction, separation, contact, alignment) and to all bodies (ajram). The author searched for a common factor (jami') between substances and those specific accidents but did not find it in color or the colored thing, as opponents claim. Since vision attaches to multiple accidents beyond color, the common factor must be more general—existence. Therefore, the judgment must be generalized to every accident that can be seen, because the common factor between the two genera (substance and accident) is existence. The argument reinforces that existence is the universal cause for visibility.

جوهر
أجرام

افتراق
مماسة
محاذاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If vision attaches to only some accidents, the cause must be something specific like color.
Correction: The author shows that vision attaches to multiple accidents beyond color, so the cause cannot be color; it must be something more general like existence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main reason for rejecting the claim that the cause of vision is color or the colored thing?
A) Color is not an accident
B) Vision attaches to many accidents beyond color
C) Color cannot be perceived
D) Color is a substance

Key Takeaway
Vision attaches to some accidents and all bodies, so its cause must be existence rather than a specific accident like color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your walk with Allah, where have you limited His power to specific 'colors'—expecting Him to act only through certain means or forms? What would it look like to trust that His sovereignty extends to every accident of your life, even the ones that seem unrelated?
?
This argument shows that a limited observation forces one to seek a deeper cause. What area of your faith are you content with surface-level explanations? Will you dig deeper until you find the true cause that unites all your experiences with Allah?
?
If existence itself is the common factor for vision, then nothing in your life is invisible to Allah. How does this knowledge change the way you live today—especially in moments you think are hidden from Him? What will you do to act on this reality right now?

Hadath Is Not a Valid Corrector for Divine Visibility
Refutes the claim that temporal origination is the corrector for divine visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal origination (hadath) cannot be the corrector for divine visibility, and how the Ash'ari response distinguishes between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot cause anything; only positive attributes can be correctors.

This passage tackles a key objection: if visibility requires temporal origination, how can God be seen? The author refutes this by showing that hadath is not a valid corrector on any view. We'll explore his two-pronged argument and the crucial distinction between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and 'musahhih'
The fourth objection claims that temporal origination (hadath) is what makes something visible. The author refutes this by analyzing the term 'musahhih' (corrector) and showing hadath cannot play that role.
A musahhih is that which renders a thing suitable for a certain judgment, like visibility.
2
Follow the argument against the Mu'tazila
The author first argues against the Mu'tazila: if hadath were a corrector, then all originated things would be visible, but some accidents are invisible by consensus. Thus hadath cannot be the corrector.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Follow the argument against al-Ash'ari's view
According to al-Ash'ari, hadath is existence preceded by non-existence. Non-existence has no causal effect, so it cannot be a corrector; only existence itself can be.
Non-existence is a pure privation.
4
Examine the rebuttal about 'particular existence'
The opponent might say that 'preceded by non-existence' means particular existence. The author responds that particularity must come from a positive attribute, not from prior non-existence. Hence existence remains the corrector.
Positive attributes include being a substance or accident, not privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الاعتراض الرابع نقول: الحدوث ليس يصلح أن يكون مصححا على المذهبين، أما على مذهبكم فلأن بعض الأعراض يستحيل رؤيته، فلو كان الحدوث مصححا لصح تعلق الرؤية بكل محدث، وأما على مذهب أبي الحسن فلأن الحدوث وجود مسبوق بعدم والعدم لا تأثير له، فبقي الوجود مصححا. وقولهم: معنى قولنا: مسبوق بعدم أنه وجود مخصوص لا وجود عام مطلق، والوجود المخصوص باعتبار الحدوث علة مصححة. قيل: الخصوص في الوجود يجب أن يكون بصفة وجودية راجعة إلى الوجود حتى يصح مؤثراً في إثبات الحكم، وسبق العدم لا يصلح أن يكون مؤثراً في إثبات الصلاحية والصحة للرؤية، فبقي الوجود المطلق، ونعني بالصفة الوجودية أن يخصص الوجود مثلاً بأنه جوهر أو عرض والعرض بأنه كون أو لون، فهذه الاعتبارات مما تؤثر فأما العدم السابق فلا تأثير له، والوجود باعتبار عدم سابق لا يكتسب اعتباراً ووجها إلا احتياجًا إلى موجد، وهو باعتبار هذا لا يصلح أن يكون مصححا للرؤية والإدراك.
As for the answer to the fourth objection: we say: temporal origination (hadath) is not valid to be a corrector (musahhih) according to both schools. As for your school (the Mu'tazila), because some accidents (a'rad) are impossible to see; if hadath were a corrector, then vision would attach to every originated thing. As for the school of Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari), because hadath is an existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect; so existence remains as the corrector. Their statement: 'the meaning of our saying 'preceded by non-existence' is that it is a particular existence, not absolute general existence, and the particular existence by virtue of hadath is a correcting cause.' It was said: particularity in existence must be by a positive attribute (sifa wujūdiyya) returning to existence so that it can be effective in establishing the judgment; and the priority of non-existence is not valid to be effective in establishing the suitability and validity for vision; so absolute existence remains. By positive attribute we mean that existence is specified, for example, as a substance or accident, and the accident as a mode (kawn) or color; these considerations are what are effective. As for the prior non-existence, it has no effect; and existence by virtue of a prior non-existence acquires no consideration or aspect except need for a creator, and by virtue of this it is not valid to be a corrector of seeing (ru'ya) and perception (idrak).
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the fourth objection against the Ash'ari position that God can be seen in the afterlife. The objection claimed that temporal origination (hadath) is what makes something visible. The author argues that hadath cannot be the corrector (musahhih) for two reasons corresponding to two schools. First, according to the Mu'tazila (who deny vision of God), some accidents are invisible by consensus (e.g., sound, smell), so if hadath were the corrector, all originated things would be visible, which is false. Second, according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, hadath is defined as existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence is a pure privation with no causal efficacy. Therefore, the effective factor can only be existence itself. Then the author addresses a possible counterclaim: that 'preceded by non-existence' refers to a particular existence (wujud makhsus) which is the correcting cause. The response is that particularity in existence must be due to a positive attribute (sifa wujudiyya) that is ontologically real and causally effective, such as being a substance or an accident. The priority of non-existence cannot confer any positive property; it merely indicates need for a creator. Hence existence as such remains the corrector, not hadath. The argument carefully avoids making existence alone sufficient for visibility (since not all existents are visible), but asserts that existence is the common factor, while additional positive attributes determine whether something is visible. The key point is that non-existence cannot be a source of any positive effect.

Key Terms
hadath
musahhih
sifa wujudiyya
ru'ya
idrak

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being preceded by non-existence (hadath) adds a positive quality to existence, making it visible.
Correction: The author clarifies that hadath is merely existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence has no causal effect. Only existence itself, or existence qualified by a positive attribute, can be the corrector.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that hadath (temporal origination) cannot be the corrector for divine visibility according to the Ash'ari view?
A) Because hadath is a positive attribute that makes things visible.
B) Because hadath is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no causal effect.
C) Because the Mu'tazila deny all visibility of God.
D) Because hadath only applies to substances, not accidents.

Key Takeaway
Hadath cannot be the corrector for divine visibility because non-existence is a privation with no causal efficacy; only existence and positive attributes can be correctors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence cannot cause anything positive, what does that say about your habit of focusing on avoiding sins rather than actively doing good? What positive attributes are you cultivating today to draw closer to God?
?
The author argues that only positive attributes, not privations, are effective. Look at your relationship with God: do you long to see Him because of His beauty and mercy, or just because you fear Hell? How can you turn your desire from a negative to a positive today?
?
You believe you will see God in the afterlife, but are you living now as if that vision matters? What one concrete act of worship or character-building will you commit to this week to make your existence more worthy of that ultimate honor?

Hearing and the Possibility of Non-Physical Perception
Clarifies the nature of hearing and posits non-physical hearing for divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the sense of hearing to argue that perception of God does not require physical contact or organs.

Core Idea
Hearing proves that perception can be non-physical, just like God's speech.

The objection that seeing God would require physical contact is flawed. The author turns to the sense of hearing to show that not all senses need contact. He then develops the concept of non-physical hearing, analogous to the Ash'ari doctrine of mental speech, to show that divine communication can occur without physical means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very logical, step-by-step argument.
2
Identify the claim about hearing
The author states that hearing, like vision, does not require contact of a body with a body. This is key to breaking the analogy that visibility implies corporeality.
Compare this with the common understanding that hearing requires sound waves and ears.
3
Understand the 'musahhih' for hearing
The author presents two opinions on what makes something audible: existence itself, or existence as speech. Both opinions allow for non-physical hearing.
This shows that the criteria for audibility are debated among scholars.
4
Learn the parallel between mental speech and mental hearing
Just as the Ash'aris established speech in the soul (kalam nafsi) without letters or sounds, the author now establishes hearing in the soul (sama' nafsi) without physical organs.
This is the crucial move to make divine perception non-physical.
5
Reflect on the Quranic proof
The verse 42:51 lists three modes of divine communication. The author interprets 'revelation' (wahy) as direct hearing without any veil, supporting the possibility of non-physical hearing.
This grounds the argument in scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الاعتراض الخامس نقول: الرؤية من جملة الحواس الخمس، لكن الحواس مختلفة الحقائق والإدراكات ولكل حاسة خاصية لا يشركها فيها غيرها، وليس منها حاسة تتعلق بجنسي الجوهر والعرض على وتيرة واحدة، بل كلها تتعلق بأعراض ومن شرط تعلقها اتصال جسم بجسم حتى يحصل ذلك الإدراك عن السمع، فإنه لا يستدعي اتصال جسم بجسم ضرورة، بل هو إدراك محض كالرؤية. ثم اختلف رأي العقلاء في أن المصحح للسمع ما هو؟ فمنهم من قال: هو الوجود كالمصحح للرؤية، ومنهم من قال المصحح كونه موجوداً باعتبار كون المسموع كلامًا، والكلام قد يكون عبارة لفظية، وقد يكون نطقا نفسيا، وكلاهما مسموعان، ونحن كما أثبتنا كلاما في النفس ليس بحرف ولا صوت، كذلك نثبت سماعًا في النفس ليس بحرف ولا صوت ثم ذلك السماع قد يكون بواسطة وحجاب، وقد يكون بغير واسطة وحجاب (ما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحيا أو من وراء حجاب أو يرسل رسولا)[الشورى: ٥١]، فالوحي ما يكون بغير واسطة وحجاب كما قال تعالى: (فَأَوْحَى إِلَى عَبْدِهِ مَا أَوْحَى)، وكما قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم...
As for the answer to the fifth objection: we say: vision (ru'ya) is one of the five senses, but the senses have different realities and perceptions, and each sense has a specificity in which no other shares it. None of the senses relates to the two genera of substance and accident in one uniform manner; rather, all of them relate to accidents, and a condition of their relation is the contact of a body with a body. However, regarding hearing (sam'), it does not necessarily require contact of a body with a body; rather, it is pure perception like vision. Then the intelligent differed about what is the corrector (musahhih) for hearing. Some said: it is existence, like the corrector for vision. Others said: the corrector is its being existent by virtue of the heard being speech (kalam), and speech may be verbal expression (ibara lafziyya) and may be mental speech (nutaq nafsi), and both are heard. And just as we have established speech in the soul (kalam nafsi) that is not letter nor sound, likewise we establish hearing in the soul (sama' nafsi) that is not letter nor sound. Then this hearing may be by means and a veil, and may be without means and veil: {It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation, or from behind a veil, or send a messenger} [al-Shura 42:51]. So revelation (wahy) is what occurs without means and veil, as the Most High said: {So he revealed to His servant what he revealed} [al-Najm 53:10], and as the Prophet, peace be upon him, said...
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of divine visibility by addressing a fifth objection that likely argued that if God is seen, He must be a body or located, analogous to other senses. The author responds by analyzing the sense of hearing (sam') as a parallel. He begins by stating that vision is one of the five senses, but the senses differ in their realities and each has a unique property. Importantly, not all senses require physical contact: hearing, like vision, does not necessarily require contact of a body with a body. This breaks the analogy that visibility implies corporeality. Then he discusses different opinions on what makes something audible (the corrector for hearing). Some say it is existence itself; others say it is existence qualified as speech. Speech can be physical (words) or mental (the internal speech behind words). Both are audible in some sense. Crucially, the author then posits a non-physical hearing (sama' nafsi) analogous to the Ash'ari concept of mental speech (kalam nafsi). Just as God's speech is not composed of letters and sounds but is an eternal attribute, so too hearing of God's speech can occur without physical mechanisms. He cites the Quranic verse 42:51 to show three modes of divine communication: revelation (wahy) without a veil, speech from behind a veil, and sending a messenger. Revelation is direct hearing without any medium. This supports the possibility of seeing God without physical encounter, as both vision and hearing can be non-physical perceptions. The argument is subtle: it does not prove that the vision of God is physical, but it undermines the objection that physical senses require physical organs and contact.

Key Terms
sam'
kalam nafsi
sama' nafsi
wahy
musahhih

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing always requires sound waves and ears.
Correction: The author argues for a purely mental hearing (sama' nafsi) that does not require physical mechanisms, analogous to mental speech.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Quranic verse 'It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation, or from behind a veil, or send a messenger' indicate?
A) That revelation is only possible through a medium.
B) That divine speech can be heard without any physical veil or medium.
C) That hearing God always requires a physical veil.
D) That vision of God is impossible because He cannot be seen physically.

Key Takeaway
The author uses the analogy of hearing to argue that divine perception can occur without physical mediation, thus defending the possibility of seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God can speak to you directly without physical means, how would you actively prepare your heart to receive that speech today?
?
The author argues that hearing can be non-physical. When was the last time you felt a deep, non-audible 'hearing' of truth in your soul, and what did it compel you to change?
?
If mental hearing is possible, why do you still limit your relationship with God to what you can physically perceive? What step will you take this week to cultivate a spiritual sensitivity beyond the five senses?

The Three Modes of Divine Speech
Enumeration of three modes of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct modes by which God communicates with His prophets.

Core Idea
Divine speech occurs in three modes: direct inspiration, from behind a veil, or through an angel.

The author begins by classifying the ways in which God's speech reaches prophets. This classification is essential for understanding the various forms of revelation and their distinctions. We will examine each mode as presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to see the three modes listed. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice that the author uses specific Quranic references.
2
Identify each mode
List the three modes: direct inspiration (nafth), from behind a veil (as with Moses), and through a messenger (angel).
3
Reflect on the distinctions
Consider why these distinctions matter for understanding how revelation occurs. What does each mode imply about God's transcendence?
The modes show that God can communicate directly or through intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفث في روعي، وقد يكون من وراء حجاب كما كان لموسى عليه السلام وكلمه ربه، وقد يكون بواسطة رسول وحجاب كما قال تعالى: (أو يرسل رسولا). وبالجملة نحن نعقل في الشاهد كلامًا في النفس ونجده من أنفسنا كما أثبتناه، ثم اللسان عنه بالعبارة الدالة عليه، ثم يسمع السامع فيصل بعد الاستماع إلى فقد وصل الكلام إلى النفس بهذه الوسائط، فلو قدرنا ارتفاع هذه الوسائط كلها من البين حتى تدرك النفس ما كانت النفس الناطقة المتكلمة مشتملة عليه، فتحقق كلام في جانب المتكلم، وسماع في جانب المستمع ولا حرف ولا صوت ولا لسان ولا صماخ، فإذا تصور مثل ذلك في الشاهد حمل استماع موسى كلام الله على ذلك، وعن هذا قال: (إني اصطفيتك على الناس برسالتي وبكلامي) [الأعراف: 144]، فالرسالات بواسطة الرسل والكلام من غير واسطة، لكنه من وراء حجاب، وأما في حقنا فكلام الله تعالى مسموع بأسماعنا، مقروء بألسنتنا، محفوظ في صدورنا، وقد تبين الفرق بين القراءة والمقروء في مسألة الكلام.
Nafth in the soul, and it may be from behind a veil as it was for Moses, peace be upon him, and his Lord spoke to him, and it may be by means of a messenger and a veil as He said: 'Or He sends a messenger.' And in summary, we comprehend in the witnessed world speech in the soul and find it in ourselves as we established, then the tongue expresses it by the expression indicating it, then the listener hears and then after listening arrives at that the speech reached the soul through these intermediaries. So if we suppose the removal of all these intermediaries from between until the soul perceives what the speaking rational soul contained, then speech is realized on the part of the speaker, and hearing on the part of the listener without letter, sound, tongue, or ear. When such is imagined in the witnessed world, the hearing of Moses of the speech of God is interpreted accordingly. And concerning this He said: 'I have chosen you over the people with My messages and My speech' [al-A'raf 144]. So the messages are through messengers, and the speech is without intermediary, but it is from behind a veil. As for our case, the speech of God Almighty is heard with our ears, read with our tongues, preserved in our chests. And the difference between the recitation and what is recited has become clear in the issue of speech.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage classifies the modes of divine speech and explains the analogy of inner speech. The author first lists three modes: direct inspiration (nafth in the soul), speech from behind a veil (as with Moses), and speech through an angel (as in revelation to prophets). Then he uses an analogy from human experience: we have inner speech (kalam nafsi) which we express with our tongues, and the listener hears it. If we remove all intermediaries, we have speech without letters or sounds. This helps understand how Moses heard God's speech without physical means. The author then distinguishes between the message (risala) which comes through angels, and the speech (kalam) which comes without intermediary but from behind a veil. For us, God's speech is accessible through recitation, hearing, and memorization, but there is a distinction between the recited words (qira'a) and the divine speech itself (maqru').

Key Terms
نفث في الروح
وراء حجاب
بواسطة رسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all revelation comes through angels.
Correction: The passage shows that God can also speak directly without an angel, as in the case of Moses.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three modes of divine speech?
A) Direct inspiration (nafth in the soul)
B) From behind a veil
C) Through a messenger
D) Through written scripture

Key Takeaway
Divine speech takes three distinct forms, each with its own mode of communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that Allah can speak directly to you through inspiration (nafth), do you actively seek His guidance in your heart, or do you rely only on external sources? What change can you make today to be more attentive?
?
The veiled speech to Moses was still a profound encounter. When you feel a sense of distance from Allah, do you still recognize His speech in your life through the 'veil' of circumstances or scripture? How can you better listen?
?
The Quran came through the angelic mode—perfectly conveyed. Do you treat the Quran as if Allah is speaking directly to you now, or do you read it as a historical text? What would change if you heard every verse as His personal address to you?

The Analogy of Inner Speech and Its Application
Analogy of inner speech and its theological application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the human experience of inner speech serves as an analogy to explain divine speech without physical attributes.

Core Idea
Inner speech in humans shows that speech can exist without letters or sounds, just as divine speech transcends physical means.

The author now uses a powerful analogy from human experience to clarify how Moses could hear God's speech without physical sounds. By reflecting on our own inner speech (kalam nafsi), we can conceive of speech that is not dependent on tongue or ear.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of inner speech
Read the Arabic passage describing how we have inner speech and then express it outwardly. Follow the reasoning step by step.
The author starts with 'wa bil-jumla' introducing the analogy.
2
Understand the removal of intermediaries
Imagine removing all physical intermediaries (tongue, ears, letters, sounds) so that only the inner speech remains. This shows that speech can be conceptual without physical form.
3
Apply to Moses' case
The author applies this analogy to Moses hearing God's speech: it was a direct reception of divine inner speech without physical sounds.
This is supported by the verse 'I have chosen you with My speech'.
4
Note the distinction between recitation and recited
The passage ends by clarifying that for us, God's speech is heard/read/memorized, but there is a difference between the physical recitation (qira'a) and the divine speech itself (maqru').
This distinction is important in the theology of the Quran's createdness.

Ash'ari Proof from Moses' Request for the Possibility of Seeing God
The author presents the Ash'ari argument that Moses' request implies the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that Moses' request to see God implies the possibility of vision, and how they refute the objection that 'lan tarani' means eternal impossibility.

Core Idea
A prophet cannot ask for the impossible; thus, seeing God must be possible.

The Ash'ari school famously argues for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife, based on the Quranic account of Moses asking to see Him. This passage presents their key proof: since Moses was a prophet aware of God's attributes, his request must have been for something possible. Moreover, God's response 'You will not see Me' does not deny possibility but only indicates it will not happen due to human weakness. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the flow from the initial dilemma to the analysis of God's response and the refutation of objections.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical dilemma
The argument begins with a dilemma: either Moses knew seeing God is possible or he did not. Examine both branches and why the second is impossible for a prophet.
This is the core of the proof - assume Moses' knowledge.
3
Analyze God's response 'lan tarani'
Notice that God did not say 'I am not visible' but 'You will not see Me.' The author argues this implies possibility because it ties the negation to the seer's capacity, not the object's nature.
The mountain example supports this interpretation.
4
Refute the 'lan' objection
The author addresses the claim that 'lan' means eternal negation. Follow the two counter-arguments: (1) 'lan' is not inherently eternal, and (2) even if it were, it negates occurrence, not possibility.
Compare with the verse about patience.
5
Summarize the argument
Now try to summarize the entire proof in your own words. Ensure you can explain why Moses' request and God's answer support the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما تمسك به الأشعري في جواز رؤية الباري جل جلاله سؤال موسى عليه السلام: (رب أرني أنظر إليك)، وجواب الرب تعالى: (لن تراني) [الأعراف: 143]. ووجه الاستدلال أن موسى عليه السلام هل كان عالما بجواز الرؤية أم كان جاهلا بذلك؟ فإن كان جاهلا فهو غير عارف بالله تعالى حق معرفته، وليس يليق ذلك بجناب النبوة، وإن كان عالما بالجواز فقد علمه على ما هو به، والسؤال بالجائز يكون لا بالمستحيل، وجواب الرب تعالى: (لن تراني) يدل على الجواز أيضا فإنه ما قال (لست بمرئي)، لكنه أثبت العجز أو عدم الرؤية من جهة الرائي، وعن هذا قال: (ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني) إذ الجبل لما لم يكن مطيقا للتجلي مع شدته وصلابته فيكف يكون البصر مطيقا؟ فربط المنع بأمر جائز، ومع جوازه أحال المنع على ضعف الآلة لا على منع الاستحالة. أليس لو كان السؤال: أرني أنظر إلى وجهك أو إلى شخصك وصورتك لم يكن الجواب بقوله: (لن تراني) بل (لست بذي شخص وصورة ووجه ومقابلة)، فدل أن السؤال كان بأمر جائز فتحقق الجواز. وإن قيل: لن للتأييد فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما أن لن للتأكيد لا للتأييد، أليس قال: (إنك لن تستطيع معي صبرا) [الكهف: 67] وهو جائز غير محال، والثاني أنه وإن كان للتأييد فليس يدل على منع الجواز، بل يدل على منع وقوع الجائز، وإنما استدللنا بالآية لإثبات الجواز وتأييد لن لا ينافيه.
Among what al-Ash'ari adhered to regarding the permissibility of seeing the Exalted Lord is the question of Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [al-A'raf 143], and the answer of the Lord: 'You will not see Me.' The point of evidence is: was Moses aware of the permissibility of seeing or ignorant of that? If he was ignorant, then he is not knowledgeable of God Almighty in His true knowledge, and that is not befitting the station of prophethood. If he was knowledgeable of the permissibility, then he knew it as it is, and the question is about the possible, not about the impossible. And the answer of the Lord: 'You will not see Me' also indicates permissibility, because He did not say 'You are not visible', but He affirmed inability or lack of seeing from the side of the seer. Concerning this He said: 'But look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' [al-A'raf 143], since the mountain was not able to bear the manifestation despite its strength and hardness, so how would the eyesight be able? So He tied the prohibition to a permissible matter, and with its permissibility He attributed the prevention to the weakness of the instrument, not to the prevention of impossibility. Would it not be that if the question were 'Show me that I may look at Your face or Your person and form', the answer would not have been 'You will not see Me' but rather 'I have no person, form, face, or confronting.' So it indicates that the question was about a permissible matter, thus permissibility is established. And if it is said: 'lan' is for eternity, it is invalid from two aspects: first, 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternity; did He not say: 'Indeed, you will never be able to have patience with me' [al-Kahf 67] which is possible, not impossible? Second, even if it were for eternity, it does not indicate prevention of permissibility, but rather prevention of the occurrence of the permissible. And we only used the verse to establish permissibility, and 'lan' for eternity does not contradict that.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya) using Moses' request in the Quran. The proof is structured as a dilemma: either Moses knew it was possible or he did not. If he did not know, he would be ignorant, which is unbefitting a prophet. So he must have known it is possible. Therefore, the request was for a possible matter. God's response 'lan tarani' (you will not see Me) does not deny possibility but only states that it will not happen, as evidenced by the condition of the mountain. If seeing God were impossible, God would have said 'I am not visible' instead of tying it to a contingent event. The passage then refutes the objection that 'lan' implies eternal negation: first, 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternity (as in other verses), and second, even if eternal, it only negates occurrence, not possibility. So the verse supports the Ash'ari position that seeing God is possible, though it will not happen in this world.

Key Terms

جواز الرؤية
لن
التأييد
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'lan tarani' as an absolute negation proving that seeing God is impossible.
Correction: The Ash'ari argue that 'lan tarani' does not deny possibility; it only states that Moses will not see God in this world due to human weakness, as shown by the mountain example and the linguistic analysis of 'lan'.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that 'lan' indicates eternal negation?
A) By arguing that 'lan' means never in Arabic
B) By citing another verse where 'lan' is used for something possible
C) By saying that possibility is not affected by eternity
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Moses' request and God's response, properly interpreted, establish that seeing God is possible though it will not occur in this life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Moses' plea 'My Lord, show me that I may look at You,' does your own heart feel a similar longing to see your Creator? If not, what is it in you that is satisfied with distance rather than intimacy?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that seeing God is possible but prevented by our weakness. If you truly believe you will see God in the afterlife, how would that certainty reshape the way you live today—your priorities, your fears, your worship?
?
God's response to Moses was not 'I am invisible' but 'you will not see Me'—pointing to human limitation. When you face the limits of your own understanding and senses in matters of faith, do you humbly accept them, or do you try to reduce God to what you can grasp?

The Ash'ari Proof from Moses' Request
Al-Shahrastani presents the Ash'ari proof from Moses' request for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the Ash'ari proof that seeing God is possible, based on Moses' request and God's response in the Quran.

Core Idea
A prophet would not ask for the impossible; Moses' request proves seeing God is possible.

Al-Shahrastani presents a classic Ash'ari argument for the possibility of seeing God, rooted in the story of Moses. The proof turns on a simple question: Could Moses, a prophet, have asked for something impossible? This lesson walks through the reasoning step by step, showing how the Quranic verse actually supports the possibility of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dialectical style: the author raises a question and answers it, then considers an objection.
The proof has two main parts: the initial argument from Moses' knowledge, and the rebuttal about the word 'lan'.
2
Understand the core dilemma
The proof begins with a dichotomy: either Moses knew seeing God is possible, or he was ignorant. If ignorant, he wouldn't truly know God; if knowledgeable, his request was valid. Recognize how this forces the conclusion that seeing God is possible.
3
Analyze God's response
God said 'You will not see Me' (lan tarani), not 'You cannot see Me.' The author argues this distinguishes impossibility from mere non-occurrence due to human weakness, as shown by the mountain analogy.
Note the contrast: God could have said 'I am not visible' but didn't.
4
Examine the objection about 'lan'
Some claim 'lan' means eternal impossibility. The author gives two counterarguments: 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternity, as in Q 18:67; and even if eternal, it only negates occurrence, not possibility.
This reinforces that the verse supports possibility, not denial of it.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما تمسك به الأشعري في جواز رؤية الباري جل جلاله سؤال موسى عليه السلام: (رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ)، وجواب الرب تعالى: (لَن تَرَانِي)، ووجه الاستدلال أن موسى عليه السلام هل كان عالما بجواز الرؤية أم كان جاهلا بذلك؟ فإن كان جاهلا فهو غير عارف بالله تعالى حق معرفته، وليس يليق ذلك بجناب النبوة، وإن كان عالما بالجواز فقد علمه على ما هو به، والسؤال بالجائز يكون لا بالمستحيل. وجواب الرب تعالى: (لَن تَرَانِي) يدل على الجواز أيضا فإنه ما قال "لست بمرئي"، لكنه أثبت العجز أو عدم الرؤية من جهة الرائي، وعن هذا قال: (وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَيْنِي) إذ الجبل لما لم يكن مطيقا للتجلي مع شدته وصلابته فيكف يكون البصر مطيقا؟ فربط المنع بأمر جائز ومع جوازه أحال المنع على ضعف الآلة لا على منع الاستحالة. أليس لو كان السؤال: "أرني أنظر إلى وجهك أو إلى شخصك وصورتك" لم يكن الجواب بقوله: (لَن تَرَانِي) بل "لست بذي شخص وصورة ووجه ومقابلة"، فدل أن السؤال كان بأمر جائز فتحقق الجواز. وإن قيل: "لن" للتأييد فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما أن "لن" للتأكيد لا للتأييد، أليس قال: (إِنَّكَ لَن تَسْتَطِيعَ مَعِيَ صَبْرًا) [الكهف:67] وهو جائز غير محال، والثاني أنه وإن كان للتأييد فليس يدل على منع الجواز، بل يدل على منع وقوع الجائز، وإنما استدللنا بالآية لإثبات الجواز وتأييد "لن" لا ينافيه.
Among what al-Ash'ari adheres to regarding the permissibility of seeing the Exalted Creator is the request of Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You,' and the response of the Lord, Exalted: 'You will not see Me.' The aspect of inference is that Moses, peace be upon him—was he knowledgeable of the permissibility of seeing, or was he ignorant of that? If he was ignorant, then he is not knowing God Almighty truly, and that is not befitting the station of prophethood. And if he was knowledgeable of the permissibility, then he knew it as it is, and the request is for what is possible, not for what is impossible. And the response of the Lord, Exalted: 'You will not see Me' also indicates permissibility, for He did not say 'You are not seen,' but He affirmed inability or lack of sight from the side of the seer. Regarding this He said: 'But look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' Since the mountain was not able to endure the manifestation despite its strength and hardness, how then can the eye endure? He thus linked the prohibition to a permissible matter, and with its permissibility He referred the prohibition to the weakness of the instrument, not to a prohibition of impossibility. If the request had been 'Show me [Yourself] that I may look at Your face or Your person and form,' the response would not have been: 'You will not see Me,' but rather 'I am not of person, form, face, and facing.' So it shows that the request was for a permissible matter, thus permissibility is established. And if it is said that 'lan' (never) indicates eternity, it is impossible from two aspects: first, that 'lan' is for emphasis, not for eternity—indeed He said: 'Indeed, you will never be able to have patience with me' [Al-Kahf:67], which is possible, not impossible. Second, even if it were for eternity, it does not indicate the prevention of permissibility, but rather indicates the prevention of the occurrence of what is permissible. And we have only inferred from the verse to establish permissibility, and the eternity of 'lan' does not contradict that.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) that seeing God (ru'ya) is possible (ja'iz), presented on behalf of the Ash'ari school. The proof is based on the story of Moses (Musa) asking to see God, as recorded in the Quran (Q 7:143). The author, al-Shahrastani, presents the argument in a dialectical form. He begins by stating that al-Ash'ari uses Moses' request as evidence. The key question is whether Moses knew that seeing God is possible or not. If he was ignorant, then he would not have truly known God, which is unworthy of a prophet. If he knew it is possible, then his request was for something possible, not impossible. Moreover, God's response 'You will not see Me' also indicates possibility because God did not say 'You are not seen,' which would deny the attribute of being visible. Instead, God attributed the inability to the seer, as shown by the analogy of the mountain: the mountain could not endure God's manifesting, so how could the eye? This links the prohibition to a permissible state, but the impossibility is only due to human weakness, not an intrinsic impossibility of God being seen. The author then considers an alternative interpretation where Moses might have asked to see God's face or form, but God would have replied differently, so the actual reply confirms the request was for a permissible vision. Finally, he refutes the claim that the word 'lan' (never) in the verse implies eternal impossibility. He gives two arguments: first, 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternal negation, as in Q 18:67 where it refers to a possible state; second, even if it were eternal, it only negates occurrence, not possibility. Thus the verse supports the possibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
جواز (jawaz)
استحالة (istihala)
لن (lan)
تجلي (tajalli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's response 'You will not see Me' (lan tarani) with the word 'lan' establishes that seeing God is eternally impossible.
Correction: The passage argues that 'lan' does not negate possibility but only occurrence, and that Moses' request itself proves the possibility of vision.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Ash'ari's proof from Moses' request?
A) That Moses was ignorant of God's nature
B) That seeing God is possible because a prophet would not ask for the impossible
C) That Moses asked on behalf of his people
D) That the word 'lan' means eternal negation

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof from Moses' request establishes that seeing God is intrinsically possible, though it may not occur due to human weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that seeing God is possible in the afterlife, how would that change your relationship with Him today? What does it mean to long for a vision of God, and do you actually desire it? Are you living in a way that reflects that hope, or have you settled for a distant, impersonal God?
?
Moses, a prophet, asked to see God. Do you have the same boldness in your dua? Or do you limit your requests to worldly matters because you doubt God's generosity? What prevents you from asking for the highest spiritual blessing—to see Him?
?
The Ash'ari proof hinges on the idea that a prophet cannot be ignorant of a matter of faith. If you claim to know God, but your actions don't reflect that knowledge, is your 'knowledge' like the hypothetical ignorance that would be unbefitting of a prophet? How can you transform your knowledge into a living reality that moves you to seek His vision?

Refuting the Claim that Moses Asked on Behalf of His People
Al-Shahrastani refutes the objection that Moses asked for vision on behalf of his people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Shahrastani argues that Moses' request to see Allah was personal and genuine, thereby supporting the Ash'ari position that seeing Allah is possible.

Core Idea
Moses' request was his own, not a rhetorical trick for his people.

The Mu'tazili objection tries to neutralize the proof from Moses' request by claiming he acted rhetorically for his people. In this passage, Al-Shahrastani dismantles this objection with multiple arguments rooted in the Quranic text itself. You will see how careful attention to context, consequences, and prophetic practice refutes the rationalist interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full argument. Note the objection first, then the author's response.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قيل' and the answer with 'قيل'.
2
Identify the first argument: contextual pronouns
Notice that the Quran uses singular pronouns when referring to Moses' request. This shows it was for himself, not the people.
The verse says 'لَن تَرَانِي' (You will not see Me) addressed to Moses alone.
3
Analyze the second argument: different fates
Compare how Moses was answered gently with a test, while his people were struck by a thunderbolt. This disparity indicates a different nature of request.
Moses got 'But look at the mountain' while the people were punished.
4
Examine the third argument: analogy with the idol request
When the people asked for an idol, Moses immediately rebuked them. If seeing Allah were impossible, he would have done the same, not engaged in dialogue.
This shows that Moses considered vision possible, unlike the idol request.
5
Reflect on delaying clarification
The Mu'tazila themselves hold that clarification cannot be delayed when needed. Here, if vision were impossible, clear denial would have come immediately, but it didn't.
This turns the Mu'tazili principle against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: سأل الرؤية لقومه لا لنفسه، وإنما سألها إلزاما عليهم بقول الله سبحانه: (لَن تَرَانِي) حيث قالوا: (أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً) [النساء:153]. قيل: هذا ما يخالف الظاهر من كل وجه، ومع مخالفته لا يجوز أن يسأل النبي سؤالا محالا لقومه، وقرينة المقال تدل على أن السؤال كان مقصورا عليه من كل وجه؛ إذ قال: (وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَيْنِي) إلى آخر الآية، ومنع موسى لا ينتهض إلزاما على القوم، بل تقرير الحجة القاطعة على أن الله تعالى لا يجوز أن يكون مرئيا يكون إلزاما، فكيف نجا موسى من السؤال بقوله: (لَن تَرَانِي, وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ) ولم ينج قومه من السؤال في قولهم: (أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً) إلا بالصاعقة المهلكة والعذاب الأليم؟ ولما قال قومه: (اجْعَل لَّنَا إِلَٰهًا كَمَا لَهُمْ آلِهَةٌ) لم يلزمهم بالسؤال عن الله تعالى، بل أجابهم في الحال: (قَوْمٌ تَجْهَلُونَ) إذ كان السؤال محالا، فكذلك الرؤية لو كانت مستحيلة لأجابهم في الحال ورفع شبههم بالمقال. ومثل هذه الحالة لو قدرتها المعتزلة ما استجازوا تأخير البيان عن وقت الحاجة، ولعدوا سؤال المسئول من مسئول آخر اتهاما آخر على شبهة قد تحققت لهم.
If it is said: He asked for the vision for his people, not for himself, and he only asked it as a refutation against them by God's saying: 'You will not see Me' — when they said: 'Show us God openly' [Q 4:153]. It is said: This contradicts the apparent meaning in every respect, and despite its contradiction, it is not permissible for the Prophet to ask an impossible question for his people. The contextual evidence indicates that the request was exclusively for him in every respect, since He said: 'But look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' to the end of the verse. And Moses' being prevented does not constitute a refutation against the people; rather, the establishment of the conclusive proof that God Almighty cannot be seen would be a refutation. How did Moses escape from the request by His saying: 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain,' while his people did not escape from their request in their saying: 'Show us God openly' except by the devastating thunderbolt and painful punishment? And when his people said: 'Make for us a god just as they have gods,' he did not impose upon them the questioning about God Almighty, but rather answered them immediately: 'You are a people who act ignorantly' — since the request was impossible. Likewise, if vision were impossible, he would have answered them immediately and removed their doubts with speech. And such a case, if the Mu'tazila had estimated it, they would not consider it permissible to delay the clarification from the time of need, and they would consider the questioning of the questioned from another questioner as another accusation regarding a doubt that has become certain to them.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an objection (i'tirad) and its answer (jawab) within the Ash'ari argument for the possibility of seeing God. The objector (likely a Mu'tazili) claims that Moses' request in the Quran was not for himself but for his people, who had asked to see God openly. Therefore, Moses' request was a rhetorical move to refute them, and the response 'You will not see Me' was directed at them. The author refutes this objection in several ways. First, the literal context of the Quranic verses contradicts this interpretation: the verses focus on Moses alone, using singular pronouns, and the command to look at the mountain is addressed to him personally. Second, if Moses were merely rebutting his people, his being denied vision would not constitute a refutation; rather, he would need to show that God cannot be seen at all. But instead, God's answer (looking at the mountain) implies possibility, as previously argued. Third, the author contrasts the fates: Moses was answered gently with 'You will not see Me' and the mountain test, whereas his people who asked to see God were struck by a thunderbolt. This disparity shows that Moses' request was of a different nature. Fourth, the author draws an analogy with another incident where the people asked for an idol; Moses immediately rebuked them, calling them ignorant, because the request was for something impossible. Similarly, if seeing God were impossible, Moses would have immediately rejected the people's request with a clear statement, not engaged in a prolonged dialogue. Finally, the author invokes the Mu'tazila's own principle that clarification cannot be delayed when needed; here, if seeing God were impossible, Moses would have clarified immediately, but he did not. Thus, the objection fails, and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
إلزام (ilzam)
شبهة (shubha)
صاعقة (sa'iqah)
تأخير البيان (ta'khir al-bayan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses asked to see Allah only as a rhetorical device for his people, not because he believed seeing Allah is possible.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani shows that the Quranic context, the different outcomes, and Moses' behavior all indicate his request was genuine and personal, implying the possibility of vision.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Al-Shahrastani gives to refute the claim that Moses asked for vision on behalf of his people?
A) He cites a different verse
B) He points out that the Quran uses singular pronouns and that Moses and his people received different outcomes
C) He argues that the Mu'tazila are always wrong
D) He claims that the people never actually asked to see God

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani demonstrates that Moses' request for vision was genuine and personal, based on contextual evidence, contrasting consequences, and prophetic behavior, thereby supporting the Ash'ari stance that seeing Allah is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever stop to consider whether your request aligns with what Allah has revealed as possible, or do you sometimes demand in a way that expects Allah to conform to your desires? How does Moses' humble request—and his acceptance of the test—challenge your own approach to asking Allah?
?
The people who demanded to see Allah openly were destroyed, while Moses asked for a similar thing and was given a sign. What does this difference reveal about the state of your own heart when you approach Allah? Are you asking out of genuine longing and submission, or out of arrogance and insistence? Answer honestly.
?
This passage refutes the idea that Moses used a rhetorical trick. It insists that a prophet's words and actions are sincere. Is there any area in your life where you are tempted to use religious language or actions as a means to an end, rather than out of genuine faith? What would it look like for you to be completely sincere before Allah in that area starting today?

Linguistic Proof from the Quranic Verse
The author analyzes 'nazara' with 'ila' to prove ru'ya is vision with eyes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the preposition used with 'nazar' determines its meaning, and how this proves that believers will see Allah with their eyes.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ila' with 'nazar' exclusively means vision, proving beatific vision.

In this passage, the author presents a linguistic analysis of the Quranic verse 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' By examining how the word 'nazar' changes meaning with different prepositions, he establishes that 'nazar ila' means direct vision. He also refutes alternative interpretations that would empty the verse of its clear meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the linguistic rule
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the different prepositions and their meanings as explained by the author.
Pay attention to the specific preposition 'ila' which is key to the argument.
2
Examine the argument
The author argues that 'nazar' with 'ila' is determined for vision, and the verse uses 'ila', so it means vision. Then he refutes the interpretation of reward.
Consider why the refutation is necessary to prevent misinterpretation.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Think about the author's statement: 'the very vision of reward is not beneficence.' How does this counter the alternative interpretation?

ومما تمسك به قوله تعالى: (وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة) [القيامة: 22-23]، والنظر إذا تعرى عن الصلات كان بمعنى الانتظار، وإذا وصل بلام كان بمعنى الإنعام، وإذا وصل بفي كان بمعنى التفكر والاستدلال، وإذا وصل بإلى تعين للرؤية، ولا يجوز حمله على الثواب فإن نفس رؤية الثواب لا يكون إنعامًا، وقد أورد النظر في معرض الإنعام واللفظ نص في رؤية البصر بعد ما نفيت عنه التأويلات الفاسدة.
And among what he adheres to is His saying, Exalted: 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Qiyamah 22–23]. And 'nazar' (looking/expectation) when it is bare of any preposition means waiting; when connected with 'lam' (li) it means beneficence; when connected with 'fi' (bi) it means reflection and inference; and when connected with 'ila' (ilā) it is determined for vision (al-ru'ya). And it is not permissible to carry it on reward, for the very vision of reward is not beneficence. And 'nazar' has been brought in the context of beneficence, and the wording is explicit for vision with the eye after false interpretations have been removed from it.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents a linguistic proof from the Quranic verse 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' The author explains the word 'nazar' with different prepositions: without a preposition it means 'waiting'; with 'lam' (li) it means 'beneficence'; with 'fi' (bi) it means 'reflection and inference'; with 'ila' (ilā) it exclusively means 'vision' (ru'ya). Since the verse uses 'ila rabbiha,' it indicates direct sight. The author also refutes the interpretation that it refers to reward, because seeing reward is itself a form of beneficence, not the beneficence itself. Thus the verse is a clear text for beatific vision.

Key Terms
نظر
إلى
رؤية
إنعام
انتظار

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'nazar' in the verse as waiting for reward or as referring to reward itself.
Correction: The author shows that with 'ila' it means vision; the reward interpretation is invalid because seeing reward is not beneficence.

Checkpoint
What does 'nazar' mean when connected with the preposition 'ila'?
A) Waiting.
B) Reflection.
C) Vision (ru'ya).
D) Beneficence.

Key Takeaway
The word 'nazar' when followed by 'ila' unequivocally means vision, and this verse is a clear proof for the beatific vision.
