Redefining Wisdom: No Obligation on God
The author questions the meaning of wisdom and redefines it to deny obligation on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists that God's wisdom does not imply any external obligation or purpose.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is simply acting according to His knowledge, not for any benefit.

The opponent argues that what reason deems good obligates God. But before accepting obligation, we must clarify what 'wisdom' means in God's case. The author provides a definition that precludes any external impeller or purpose, and from there it follows that requital is not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and the author's initial question
Notice how the author does not immediately reject the claim but asks for clarification of key terms like 'wisdom' and 'wise'.
2
Understand the author's definition of wisdom
The author defines wisdom as the occurrence of an act in accordance with knowledge, regardless of benefit or purpose. This definition is crucial because it removes any external motive.
The author emphasizes that God has no impeller or higher motive.
3
Apply the definition to the case of the non-owner
The author uses the analogy of a person doing a praiseworthy act without the owner's permission to show that requital is not obligatory, especially since the owner does not benefit.
For God, doing or not doing is equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم: ما يحسن من العقل يحسن من حيث الحكمة فيجب على الله. قلنا: ما المعنى بقولكم: يجب على الله تعالى من حيث الحكمة؟ وما معنى الحكيم والحكمة؟ فإن عندنا وقوع الفعل على حسب العلم حكمة، سواء كان فيه مصلحة وغرض أو لم يكن، بل لا حامل للمبدع الأول على ما يفعله، فلو كان فالحامل فوقه والداعي أعلى منه بل فعله وصنعه على هيئة يحصل نظام الموجودات بأسرها من غير أن يكون له حامل من خارج وغرض وداع من الغير، والحكيم من فعل فعلا على مقتضى علمه، والحسن والأحكام في الفعل من آثار العلم، وأما الغير إذا فعل فعلا مستحسنًا عنده من غير إذن المالك فليس من الحكمة وجوب المجازاة على ذلك الفعل، خصوصا والمالك لم ينتفع بذلك المستحسن، ولا اكتسب زينة وجمالا، والحال عنده إن فعل وإن لم يفعل على وتيرة واحدة. وبقي أن يقال: إذا لم يرجع إلى المالك نفع ولا ضرر فربما يرجع إلى الفاعل فيعارض بأن يقال: ذلك الفعل في الحال مشقة وكلفة مع جواز أن يخطئ فيعاقب في ثاني الحال حساب وكتاب مع جواز أن يثاب على الصواب، فأي عقل يخاطر هذه المخاطرة ويقتحم هذا الاقتحام، وإن رجعوا إلى عادات الناس في شكر المنعم والثناء على المحسن، والتعبد للمالك والعباد للملوك أنها من مستحسنات العقول. والجواب عنه من وجهين: أحدهما: أن العادة لا تكون دليلا عقليًا يلزم الحكم به ضرورة، بل العادات متعارضة، والشكر والكفر سيان في حق من لا ينتفع بشكر ولا يتضرر بكفر.
And their saying: 'What is good according to reason is good in terms of wisdom, so it is obligatory upon God.' We say: What do you mean by your saying: 'It is obligatory upon God Almighty in terms of wisdom'? And what is the meaning of 'the wise' and 'wisdom'? For with us, the occurrence of the act in accordance with knowledge is wisdom, whether there is benefit and purpose in it or not. Rather, the First Originator has no impeller to what He does. If there were, then the impeller would be above Him and the motive higher than Him. Rather, His act and His making are in a manner that brings about the whole system of existents without there being for Him any external impeller, purpose, or motive from another. The wise is one who does an act in accordance with His knowledge, and goodness and perfection in the act are effects of knowledge. As for another, if he does a praiseworthy act without the permission of the owner, it is not from wisdom that requital for that act is obligatory, especially since the owner did not benefit from that praiseworthy act nor acquired adornment and beauty, while the situation with Him is the same whether He does it or does not. It remains to be said: If no benefit or harm reverts to the owner, perhaps it reverts to the doer. This is countered by saying: That act is in the present moment toil and trouble, with the possibility that he errs and is punished in the next moment—account and book—with the possibility that he is rewarded for correctness. So what intellect would risk this risk and plunge into this plunge? And if they appeal to the customs of people in thanking the benefactor, praising the good-doer, worshipping the owner, and serving kings, that these are among the approvals of intellects. The answer comes from two aspects: One: Custom is not a rational proof that necessitates judgment necessarily; rather, customs conflict, and gratitude and ingratitude are equal in the case of one who does not benefit from gratitude nor suffer from ingratitude.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a refutation-and-answer sequence. The author first presents an opponent's claim: whatever reason deems good is also good in terms of wisdom, and thus God is obligated to do it. The author responds by questioning the meaning of 'wisdom' and 'the wise.' For the author, wisdom is simply God's act occurring in accordance with His knowledge, with no external purpose or benefit. There is no impeller on God. If there were, that impeller would be above Him. God's act produces the entire cosmos without any external motive. The wise person is one who acts according to his knowledge; goodness and perfection are effects of knowledge. If a non-owner does a praiseworthy act without the owner's permission, requital is not obligatory, especially since the owner does not benefit. For God, doing or not doing is equal. The author then presents an objection: even if no benefit or harm accrues to the owner (God), it might accrue to the doer (the human engaging in theology). The doer faces toil now and punishment later if wrong, so no rational person would risk it. If the opponent appeals to social customs (thanking benefactors, etc.), the author gives two answers, but only the first appears in the window: custom is not a rational proof; customs conflict, and gratitude and ingratitude are equal toward God, who is unaffected.

Key Terms
حكمة
حكيم
وجوب
مصلحة
غرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's wisdom requires acting for a benefit or purpose.
Correction: The author states wisdom is the act according to knowledge, regardless of benefit or purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct definition of God's wisdom?
A) Acting for the benefit of creation
B) Acting according to knowledge with no purpose
C) Acting for a purpose
D) Acting according to custom

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom is defined as the occurrence of the act in accordance with His knowledge, with no external impeller or purpose, and therefore there is no rational basis for asserting obligation upon Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's wisdom does not require benefit or purpose, how will you stop searching for hidden 'reasons' behind your trials and instead trust that His act flows from pure knowledge? What would it take for you to truly surrender the need to see the good in every hardship?
?
The passage says God has no impeller above Him. Have you been acting as if your good deeds can compel God's favor or that your sins push Him away? What would change in your worship if you believed nothing can obligate Him?
?
You often ask 'Why did God allow this?' as if wisdom demands an explanation you can understand. After this lesson, can you commit to saying 'I do not know, but His act is wise because it is from His knowledge'? What would it mean to live in that silence?

Answering the Objection: No Rational Risk is Worth It
The author addresses the objection and provides the first answer: custom is not a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why appealing to custom is not a valid rational proof for God's obligation.

Core Idea
Custom is not a rational proof; gratitude and ingratitude are equal toward God.

After denying obligation from wisdom, the author anticipates an objection: maybe the doer (the theologian) benefits? He shows that the risk of error and punishment outweighs any potential benefit. Then he addresses the appeal to custom, arguing that custom is not a reliable rational basis.

Guided Path
1
Follow the objection about benefit to the doer
The objector says that even if no benefit goes to God, perhaps the doer benefits. The author counters that the doer faces toil now and risk of punishment later, so no rational person would take that risk.
2
Understand the appeal to custom
The opponent might appeal to social customs like thanking benefactors. The author is ready to answer this.
3
Grasp the first answer
The author says custom is not a rational proof necessitating judgment; customs conflict, and for God, gratitude and ingratitude are equal because He does not benefit or suffer.
This is the first of two answers.

Why Gratitude Cannot Obligate God
The author argues against human deserving establishing divine obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human acts of gratitude cannot establish a moral obligation on God, according to the author's argument from proportionality.

Core Idea
No human act can equal God's blessings, so gratitude creates no divine debt.

This passage tackles a common intuition: if someone is grateful, God should reward them. The author deconstructs this by showing that even if we accept custom as a basis for obligation, the required proportionality between reward and deed is impossible when comparing finite human actions to infinite divine blessings. You will see why the notion of deserving divine reward collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'then if one concedes...'
2
Follow the argument's two parts
The author first states that even slight ingratitude shows deficiency, so no deserved reward. Then he concedes custom for argument's sake and shows that custom requires equal exchange, which is impossible between finite and infinite.
The second part is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms استحقاق (deserving), وجوب (obligation), تعاوض (compensation), and قدر (quantity). Understand how they function in the argument.
These terms are central to the theological discussion of divine justice.
4
Consider the conclusion
The author concludes that neither reason nor custom can establish an obligation on God. Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجب عليه فعلا، ولا إذا قابل نعمة بشكر يسير كان الثواب واجبا عليه؛ لأنه يعد مقصرا في أداء شكر ما أنعم عليه، فكيف يستحق نعمة أخرى؟ فمن هذا الوجه لا يثبت قط استحقاق العبد ولا يتحقق قط وجوبه على الرب. ثم إن سلم تسليم جدل أنه يستحسن من حيث العادة الشكر على النعم، فكذلك يستحسن من حيث العادة أن يتقابل العوضان ويتماثل الأمران قدرا وزمانا، ولا يتصور أن يقع التقابل والتماثل بين أكثر كثير النعم وبين أقل قليلها، ولا يتحقق التعاوض بين سرمدي الوجود دواما وبين ما لا يثبت له وجود إلا لحظة أو خطرة أو لفظة، كما يقتضي العقل إيجاب شيء في مقابلة شيء مكافأة وجزاء، كذلك يقتضي اعتبار قدر الشيء في مقابلة قدر وبالاتفاق لم يعتبر القدر فيجب أن لا يعتبر الأصل، فإن كنا نستفيد الوجوب من العقل ونعتبر العقل بالعادة، فهذا هو قضية العقل والعادة صدقا، فكيف يثبت مثله استحقاق العبد وجوبا على الله تعالى؟
It is obligatory upon him actually, and if he compares a blessing with a slight gratitude, the reward would be obligatory upon him because he is considered deficient in performing gratitude for what was bestowed upon him, so how can he deserve another blessing? So from this perspective, the servant's deserving is never proven, nor is its obligation upon the Lord ever realized. Then if one concedes for the sake of argument that it is considered good according to custom to be grateful for blessings, then likewise it is considered good according to custom that the two compensations correspond and the two matters are equal in quantity and time, and it is inconceivable that correspondence and equality occur between the most abundant of blessings and the least of them, nor is compensation realized between that which is eternal in existence perpetually and that whose existence is only for a moment or an instant or a word, just as reason necessitates imposing something in exchange for something as recompense and reward, likewise it necessitates considering the quantity of a thing in exchange for quantity, and by agreement quantity was not considered, so the principle should not be considered. For if we derive obligation from reason and we measure reason by custom, then this is the judgment of reason and custom truly, so how can the servant's deserving of an obligation upon God be established?
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a proof that human deserving cannot establish an obligation on God. It begins by stating the problem: if one compares a small blessing with a slight thankfulness, one would think reward is obligatory, but that would imply the servant is deficient, so how can he deserve another blessing? The author then makes a concession: if we accept custom as a basis, custom requires proportionality in exchange. But there is no proportionality between God's infinite blessings and man's finite acts. Hence the basis of obligation fails. The author concludes that relying on reason and custom cannot establish an obligation on God.

Key Terms

وجوب
تعاوض
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a person is grateful, God owes them reward.
Correction: This passage shows that gratitude cannot obligate God because no human act is proportionate to divine blessings.

Checkpoint
Why cannot custom establish an obligation on God according to the passage?
A) Because custom is not a valid source of obligation.
B) Because custom would require equal exchange, which is impossible between finite and infinite.
C) Because reason alone suffices for obligation.
D) Because God is not bound by any rule.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude and human actions cannot impose an obligation on God because no finite act can match infinite divine blessings in proportion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I've been praying regularly, so God should answer my dua' or 'I gave charity, so God must bless my wealth'? How does this passage challenge the expectation that your actions create a debt for God? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you abandoned that sense of entitlement entirely?
?
The author says even your gratitude for blessings is deficient. When you look at your own worship, do you honestly feel it could ever be enough? Does that realization lead you to despair, or does it drive you to rely more on God's mercy and generosity rather than your own deserving?
?
If you truly believed that no act of yours can obligate God, would your motivation for good deeds change? What would it look like to serve God not to earn reward but out of pure love and gratitude for His infinite blessings? What is one concrete step you can take today to shift your intention away from expecting a return?

Obligation on the Servant Is Not from Reason or Custom
Author argues obligation is not from reason or custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God's independence from creation means worship is not rationally obligatory.

Core Idea
God has no need—so worship cannot be a rational obligation.

This passage challenges a common assumption: that worship is rationally required of every person. The author argues that since God is utterly independent—neither harmed by sin nor benefited by obedience—there is no rational basis for imposing worship. Instead, He could simply grant blessings freely. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold in your mind.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end—it's a key move.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that obligation is not from reason or custom. Find the core reason given for this claim.
Look for the phrase 'if He is not harmed...'
3
Follow the logical structure
Trace the argument: (1) God has no need from worship, (2) He can give blessings without it, (3) so imposing burdensome worship is not rational. Consider the rhetorical question as a conclusion.
The argument uses a conditional (if...then) backed by a rhetorical question.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Many think worship is good and thus obligatory. But the passage argues that goodness alone does not create obligation because God's independence removes any ground for demanding worship.
This ties back to the core idea: God's needlessness nixes rational obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوب على العبد والاستحقاق الله تعالى فليس من قضايا العقل والعادة أيضا، فإنه إذا لم يتضرر بمعصية ولا ينتفع بطاعة، ولم تتوقف قدرته في الإيجاد على فعل واحد وحال يصدر من الغير فيستعين به، بل كما أنعم ابتداء قدر على الإنعام دواما كيف يوجب على العبد عبادة شاقة في الحال لارتقاب ثواب في ثاني الحال، أليس لو رمي إليه زمام الاختيار حتى يفعل ما يشاء جريا على نسق طبيعته المائلة إلى لذيذ الشهوات، ثم أجزل في العطاء من غير حساب، كان ذلك أروح للعبد وما كان قبيحا عند العقلاء
As for the obligation upon the servant and the deserving of God, it is not among the judgments of reason and custom either. For if He is not harmed by disobedience nor does He benefit from obedience, and His power in creation does not depend on any act or state from another so that He would seek help from it, but just as He bestowed originally, He is able to bestow perpetually, how can He impose a burdensome worship upon the servant in the present in anticipation of a reward in the future? Is it not that if He handed over the reins of choice so that he does as he pleases, following the course of his nature inclined to pleasurable desires, then He gave generously without account, that would be more soothing for the servant and would not be considered ugly by rational people?
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the obligation upon the servant is not derived from reason or custom. It points out that God does not suffer harm from sin nor benefit from obedience, and His power is independent of any human action. Since He can give blessings without any condition, imposing a difficult worship in exchange for future reward seems unjustified. The passage suggests that it would be more merciful to leave people to their natural inclinations and then give generously, and that this would not be considered bad by rational people.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الاستحقاق
تكليف
عادة
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worship is good in itself and therefore rationally obligatory.
Correction: Goodness alone does not impose obligation because God has no need of worship and can bestow blessings unconditionally, so reason does not demand worship.

Checkpoint
Why is it not rational to impose worship on the servant according to the passage?
A) Because God does not need anything from His creation.
B) Because worship is not good.
C) Because the servant does not have free will.
D) Because reason is not a source of obligation.

Key Takeaway
Because God is completely independent and can give blessings without condition, worship is not a rational obligation upon humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you feel you are paying a debt to God, or accepting a gift? The author says God does not need your obedience—how does that truth shape your intention when you stand before Him?
?
If God could give you everything without you ever praying or fasting, yet He commands it, do you see worship as a burden or an invitation? What would you choose if the reins were in your hands?
?
The author says that if God gave you freedom to follow your desires and still rewarded you generously, that would be more soothing. Why does your heart resist that thought? Are you comfortable with a God who doesn't need your worship?

The Eighteenth Principle: God Acts Without Cause or Purpose
Sanusi states the Ash'ari doctrine rejecting any cause or purpose in God's acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God's acts are uncaused and without purpose, and why this is central to divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's creative act has no cause—He is the uncaused cause of all else.

This passage lays down a foundational principle of Ash'ari theology: God is absolutely transcendent and His acts are not subject to external motives or purposes. Before we explore the various related concepts (benefit, grace, etc.), Sanusi asserts the core doctrine clearly. Read carefully to grasp the logical structure of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the strong opening statement and the list of topics that will be refuted.
The opening enumeration gives a preview of the entire principle.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states the core doctrine: 'God created the world ... without any cause impelling Him.' Understand the negation of purpose (gharaḍ) and cause (ʿilla).
The phrase 'لا لعلة حاملة له' is key.
3
Analyze the two rejections
The passage rejects two ways of imagining a cause: beneficial to God (impossible) or beneficial to creatures (not a motive). Ask why each is rejected.
God does not accept benefit or harm; creatures' benefit does not move Him.
4
Grasp the concluding maxim
The final sentence 'The cause of everything is His making, and there is no cause for His making' summarizes the entire doctrine. Memorize it.
This maxim distinguishes Ash'ari theology from Mu'tazilite and other views.
5
Connect to related concepts
The opening list mentions benefit (ṣalāḥ), grace (luṭf), etc. For now, note that all these will be defined later in light of this core principle.
This lesson focuses only on the principle itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثامنة عشرة في إبطال الغرض والعلة في أفعال الله تعالى وإبطال القول بالصلاح والأصلح واللطف، ومعنى التوفيق والخذلان والشرح والحتم والطبع، ومعنى النعمة والشكر، ومعنى الأجل والرزق. مذهب أهل الحق أن الله تعالى خلق العالم بما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض وأصناف الخلق والأنواع لا لعلة حاملة له على الفعل، سواء قدرت تلك العلة نافعة له أو غير نافعة؛ إذ ليس يقبل النفع والضر، أو قدرت تلك العلة نافعة للخلق؛ إذ ليس يبعثه على الفعل باعث، فلا غرض له في أفعاله ولا حامل، بل علة كل شيء صنعه ولا علة لصنعه.
The eighteenth principle: On invalidating purpose and cause in the acts of God Most High, and invalidating the statement of [divine] benefit, the most beneficial, and grace, and the meaning of granting success, abandonment, expansion, decree, and nature, and the meaning of blessing and gratitude, and the meaning of term and provision. The doctrine of the People of Truth is that God Most High created the world, including what it contains of substances, accidents, kinds of creatures, and species, without any cause impelling Him to the act, whether that cause is imagined to be beneficial to Him or not beneficial, since He does not accept benefit or harm; or whether that cause is imagined to be beneficial to the creation, since there is no motive impelling Him to the act. So there is no purpose in His acts and no impeller. Rather, the cause of everything is His making, and there is no cause for His making.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a statement of doctrine. It opens by naming the eighteenth principle and listing its topics: the refutation of purpose and cause in God's actions, and the refutation of the doctrines of benefit (salāh), the most beneficial (aslah), and grace (lutf), along with definitions of related terms. Then it presents the Ash'arī position (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq). The author asserts that God created the entire world without any cause that would impel Him to act, whether the cause is conceived as beneficial to Himself or not, since He is above benefit or harm. Even if the cause is imagined to benefit creatures, still there is no motive (bā'ith) impelling Him. Thus, there is no purpose (gharaḍ) in His acts and no impeller (ḥāmil). The final sentence is key: 'The cause of everything is His making (ṣun'uhu), and there is no cause for His making.' In other words, God's creative act is uncaused; everything other than God is caused by His act, but His act itself has no external cause.

Key Terms
غرض
علة
باعث
حامل
صنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'no cause' means God's acts are arbitrary or without wisdom.
Correction: Ash'ari theology holds that God acts by His perfect will, which is not subject to external constraints; His wisdom is inherent, not dependent on purposes.

Checkpoint
According to the People of Truth, why does God create the world?
A) For a purpose beneficial to Himself
B) For a purpose beneficial to creatures
C) Without any cause or purpose
D) For a necessary reason

Key Takeaway
God's act of creation is uncaused; He is the sole cause of everything else, and His making has no external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not act for any benefit to Himself or to you, does that change why you think He created you? Do you ever assume that your good deeds obligate God to reward you? How would your worship change if you truly believed that God owes you nothing and that every blessing is purely from His uncaused will?
?
The passage says there is no motive impelling God to act. Have you ever felt that your prayers or efforts 'move' God or 'cause' Him to do something? What would it mean to trust that God's will is completely free and not swayed by anything you do—while still believing that He hears and answers?
?
Your very existence has no cause outside of God's act. If you truly internalized that, would you live differently today? What would it look like to live with the constant awareness that you are here solely because God willed without any reason? Does that idea make you feel insignificant or profoundly valued? How will you respond?

The Philosophers' Definition of True Generosity
The philosophers define true generosity and apply it to the First Principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of generosity as bestowal without compensation or purpose, and how they apply this to the First Principle.

Core Idea
True generosity gives what is appropriate without expecting anything in return.

The philosophers argue that all human giving is tainted by self-interest, while divine generosity is pure, free from any purpose or return. This passage lays out their definition and claims about the First Principle, distinguishing between transactional giving and true generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the key distinctions
Read the passage carefully, focusing on how the philosophers divide the bestowal of good into types: one with a benefit returning to the giver, and the other without.
Notice the contrast between exchange (مقابلة المال بالمال) and giving for reward or virtue.
2
Identify the definition of generosity
Find where they explicitly define generosity (الجود والإنعام) as giving what is appropriate without compensation or purpose.
The phrase 'بلا عوض وغرض' is key.
3
Apply to the First
Understand how they claim the First has bestowed generosity upon all existents appropriately, without any deficiency or withholding due to need.
They argue that any imagined alternative would be a deficiency, not perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول، على الفعل، والعالي لا يريد أمرًا لأجل السافل، بل الأفعال صدرت عن المبادئ وهي استندت إلى العقل الفعال، وإنما أبدع العقل بلوازمه، وأبدع بتوسطه سائر الأشياء على وجه اللزوم عنه ضرورة ؛ إذ ليس يتصور وجود واجب الوجود إلا كذلك.
قالوا : إفادة الخير والصلاح من المنعم تنقسم إلى ما يكون لفائدة وغرض يرجع إلى المفيد، والفائدة تنقسم إلى ما هو مثل المبذول كمقابلة المال بالمال، وإلى ما ليس مثلا كمن يبذل المال رجاء للثواب والمحمدة أو اكتساب صفة الفضيلة وطلب الكمال به، وهذه أيضًا معاوضة وليس بجود، كما أن الأول معاملة وليس بإنعام بل الجود والإنعام هو إفادة ما ينبغي من غير عوض وغرض، فالأول قد أفاد الجود على الموجودات كلها كما ينبغي على ما ينبغي من غير ادخار ممكن من ضرورة أو حاجة أو رقبة ؛ بحيث لو قدر غيره من الممكنات الوهمية كان نقصا في ذلك الموضوع لا كمالا ، فكل ذلك بلا عوض ولا فائدة، وغيره يتصور أمراً ثم يعرض له احتياج إلى وجوده فيحتال لوجوده ليتم غرضه به ؛ كمن يريد يبني دارا تصورها أولا ثم احتاج إليها للاستكنان والسكنى احتال لوجودها آلاتها ليتم غرضه بها، أو من يهب مالا أو يعطي عطاء ابتداء أو عقيب سؤال يتصور في نفسه أولا اكتساب حمد وجزاء، وحمله على ذلك ضعف حال الفقير فرق له رقة الجنسية، فأنعم عليه، كان الحامل له على النوال نفع يلحقه أو ضرر يدفعه، وتعالى الخالق الأول عن ذلك، ولم يكن تصوره وعلمه من المتصور المعلوم حتى يكون هو الحامل، بل التصور من العلم والمقدور من القدرة، هذا كلام القوم
وهو حسن لولا تشبيههم بأمور لا نرتضيها، والتزامهم أمورًا لا نستقصيها.
First, concerning action: the High does not will a thing for the sake of the low. Rather, actions proceed from principles, and they are based on the Active Intellect. The Intellect originated with its concomitants, and through its mediation all other things originated necessarily by necessity from it, since the existence of the Necessary Existent cannot be conceived except so. They said: The bestowal of good and benefit from the beneficent is divided into what is for a benefit and purpose returning to the benefactor. And the benefit is divided into what is like the bestowed, such as exchanging money for money, and into what is not like it, such as one who gives money hoping for reward and praise or acquiring the attribute of virtue and seeking perfection through it. This too is a transaction and not generosity, just as the first is a dealing and not a bestowal. Rather, generosity and bestowal are the giving of what is appropriate without compensation and purpose. The First has bestowed generosity upon all existents as is appropriate, in the manner that is appropriate, without any possible withholding due to necessity, need, or hope; such that if one imagined other possible things, it would be a deficiency in that subject, not a perfection. So all that is without compensation and without benefit. Another (being) conceives a thing, then a need arises for its existence, so he contrives for its existence to fulfill his purpose through it—like one who wants to build a house, first conceives it, then needs it for shelter and dwelling, so he contrives for the existence of its tools to fulfill his purpose through it. Or one who bestows wealth or gives a gift initially or in response to a request, first conceives in himself acquiring praise and reward, and what impels him to that is the weak state of the poor, so he feels compassion due to kinship, and bestows upon him. The impeller for the gift is a benefit that reaches him or a harm that he repels. Exalted is the First Creator above that. His conception and knowledge are not from the conceived known such that it would be the impeller; rather, conception is from knowledge and the predestined from power. This is the speech of the people. And it is good, were it not for their likening (God) to things we do not approve, and their committing to things we do not fully investigate.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the philosophers' argument concerning divine action and generosity. It begins by stating that the First Principle does not will anything for the sake of something lower; rather, acts proceed from principles and the Active Intellect emanates necessarily. Then they divide the bestowal of good into that which is for a benefit returning to the giver and that which is not. They argue that all human giving is transactional—either like exchange or seeking reward—while true generosity is giving what is appropriate without compensation or purpose. The First has bestowed generosity upon all existents appropriately, without any need or hope, such that any imagined alternative would be a deficiency. This giving is without compensation or benefit. In contrast, other beings conceive of something, then need it, and contrive for its existence to fulfill a purpose, like building a house. Or they give out of pity or hope of praise. God is exalted above that; His conception and knowledge are not derived from the conceived known such that it would be a motive; rather, conception is from knowledge and the predestined from power. The author then comments that this argument is good except for their comparisons and commitments that are not acceptable.

Key Terms
الجود
الإنعام
العوض
الغرض
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: True generosity allows for some purpose or return, just not a material one.
Correction: The philosophers explicitly define true generosity as being without any compensation or purpose (بلا عوض وغرض). Any benefit returning to the giver, even reward or virtue, makes it a transaction, not generosity.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what distinguishes true generosity from a transaction?
A) It is done out of pity.
B) It is without compensation or purpose.
C) It involves a return to the giver.
D) It seeks reward.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define true divine generosity as bestowal without any purpose, compensation, or need, in contrast to all human giving which is transactional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine your last significant act of giving. Was there even a hidden expectation—a thank you, recognition, or self-satisfaction? How does the philosophers' definition of generosity expose the conditionality of your heart?
?
If God's giving is without any need or purpose returning to Him, why do you so often serve Him as if He needs your obedience or as if you are doing Him a favor? What does that reveal about your understanding of His perfection?
?
The passage says any withholding from the First would be a 'deficiency'. When you withhold generosity from others—time, forgiveness, resources—what does that deficiency say about your own sense of perfection and need?

Critique of the Philosophers' Divine Action
The author presents and critiques the philosophers' extrapolation from human motivation to divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of the philosophers' argument, specifically their problematic analogies and metaphysical commitments.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument is plausible, but their analogies liken God to deficient beings.

After presenting the philosophers' definition of generosity, the author adds a critical note. He acknowledges the argument's strength but rejects their anthropomorphic comparisons and dubious metaphysical commitments. This passage details their examples of human action and the author's objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of human giving
The philosophers give examples: building a house for a purpose, giving out of pity for praise. Identify each example and its motivation.
These contrast with the First's action, which has no purpose or need.
2
Understand the critique
The author says the philosophers' speech is good except for their 'likening to things we do not approve' and 'commitments we do not fully investigate.'
He does not specify the exact objections here.
3
Note the final point
The author adds that God's conception and knowledge are not derived from the conceived such that they would be motives; rather, conception is from knowledge and predestination from power.
This hints at a different account of divine knowledge and action.

Source Text

وهو حسن لولا تشبيههم بأمور لا نرتضيها، والتزامهم أمورًا لا نستقصيها.
First, concerning action: the High does not will a thing for the sake of the low. Rather, actions proceed from principles, and they are based on the Active Intellect. The Intellect originated with its concomitants, and through its mediation all other things originated necessarily by necessity from it, since the existence of the Necessary Existent cannot be conceived except so. And the benefit is divided into what is like the bestowed, such as exchanging money for money, and into what is not like it, such as one who gives money hoping for reward and praise or acquiring the attribute of virtue and seeking perfection through it. Rather, generosity and bestowal are the giving of what is appropriate without compensation and purpose. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' examples prove that God acts with purpose, just less selfishly.
Correction: The examples are meant to show that all human action has a motive (benefit or harm), whereas God is above any need or purpose. The critique is that the philosophers still liken God to such beings by implication.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the philosophers' argument in this passage?
A) It is entirely false.
B) It is good except for their inappropriate comparisons and commitments.
C) It fails to define generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your worship, do you ever find yourself acting as if God is like a human who needs to be impressed or appeased? When you pray for a specific outcome, are you seeking to influence God's decisions rather than surrendering to His will? Examine one recent prayer and ask: Did my attitude imply a human-like cause-and-effect in God's response? How can you purify your belief to align with the author's point that God is exalted above such motives?
?
Think of a time when you were convinced by an argument about God that later turned out to be based on an inappropriate human analogy. What was that argument, and how did you come to see its flaw? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of likening God to created beings, even in seemingly plausible reasoning?
?
The author says the philosophers' speech is 'good' except for their analogies. How often do you accept a seemingly good argument about God without scrutinizing whether it makes Him too similar to His creation? Identify one belief you currently hold about God's actions—such as why He answers prayers or tests people—and honestly assess whether that belief is rooted in revelation or in a human analogy. What step will you take to correct it if needed?

The Mu'tazila Argument for Divine Purpose
Mu'tazila argue God's wisdom necessitates purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational and scriptural argument that God's wisdom necessitates acting for a purpose and benefit.

Core Idea
God's wisdom means His actions are never random but always purposeful.

The Mu'tazila present a reasoned objection against the position that God acts without purpose. They claim that both reason and revelation show that God, being wise, must act with precision and intent. This passage lays out their case, beginning with a definition of wisdom and concluding with a scriptural support.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's opening claim
Begin by reading the first sentence where they state that proof has established that God is wise. Notice how they set up their argument from reason.
This is the foundation of their entire objection.
2
Understand the definition of 'wise'
They define the wise person as one whose actions are based on precision and perfection. This definition leads to the conclusion that God's actions cannot be haphazard.
The term 'hakim' implies a standard of purposeful action.
3
Examine the division of benefit
The passage divides benefit into what returns to the agent (if needy) or to others. The example of rescuing someone without reward shows that beneficial action can be for others, not just self-interest.
This distinction is crucial to rebut the idea that God might benefit from creation.
4
Consider the scriptural support
The Mu'tazila quote a scripture: 'I did not create creation for profit from them, but rather I created them that they may profit from Me.' This reinforces that God's purpose is to benefit creatures.
They use revelation to bolster their rational argument.
5
Reflect on the overall objection
Think about how this argument challenges the Ahl al-Haqq position. The Mu'tazila conclude that wisdom in creation is apparent to reason and mentioned in revelation.
This sets up the need for a response from the Ahl al-Haqq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: قد قام الدليل على أن الرب تعالى حكيم، والحكيم من تكون أفعاله على إحكام وإتقان، فلا يفعل فعلا جزافًا، فإن وقع خيراً فخير، وإن وقع شرا فشر، بل لا بد أن ينجو غرضا ويقصد صلاحًا ويريد خيرا، ثم إن النفع ينقسم إلى ما يرجع إلى الفاعل إن كان محتاجًا إليه أو إلى غيره، وإن كان الفاعل غنيًّا غير محتاج كإنقاذ الغرقى وتخليص الهلكى مستحسن في العقل، وربما لا يكون المنقذ مكتسبا نفعًا ومتوقعاً حمدًا أو أجرًا، وعن ذلك ورد في بعض الكتب: ما خلقت الخلق لا ربح عليهم بل خلقتهم ليربحوا علي. والذي يقرره أن الحكمة في خلق العالم ظاهرة لمن تأملها بالعقل، منصوصة طلبها في السمع.
The Mu'tazila said: The proof has been established that the Lord, exalted is He, is wise. The wise one is one whose actions are based on precision and perfection, so he does not do an action haphazardly. Then whether it occurs good or evil, good or evil, but rather he must achieve a purpose, intend benefit, and desire good. Then benefit is divided into that which returns to the agent if he is in need of it, or to others. And if the agent is wealthy, not in need, like rescuing the drowning and saving the perishing, it is considered good in the intellect, and perhaps the rescuer does not acquire benefit nor expect praise or reward. And regarding that it is related in some scriptures: 'I did not create creation for profit from them, but rather I created them that they may profit from Me.' And what he establishes is that the wisdom in creating the world is apparent to one who contemplates it with reason, and it is textually mentioned, sought in revelation.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Mu'tazila against the claim that God acts without purpose. It begins by stating that reason proves God is wise (hakim). The wise person's actions are precise and perfect, not random. Therefore, God's actions must have purpose and benefit. The argument then divides benefit: it can return to the agent (if needy) or to others. Even if the agent is not in need, like a person rescuing someone without expecting reward, the act is rationally good. The Mu'tazila support this with a scriptural quote: 'I did not create creation for profit from them, but rather I created them that they may profit from Me'—implying that God's creation brings benefit to creatures. Finally, the passage asserts that the wisdom in creation is evident to reason and also mentioned in revelation. This objection challenges the earlier Ahl al-Haqq position by asserting that God's wisdom necessitates purposive action.

إحكام
إتقان
جزاف
غرض
نفع
حكمة
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila claim that God benefits from His actions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that benefit can be for others, not necessarily for God. They use the example of rescuing the drowning without personal gain.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what does the wise person's action necessarily involve?
A) It must be haphazard
B) It must have a purpose and benefit
C) It must avoid any benefit
D) It must be for the agent's own gain

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that God's wisdom necessitates that His actions have purpose and benefit, supported by both reason and scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action of God is precisely designed for some purpose, would you still complain about the trials in your life? How does this truth challenge your reaction to recent difficulties you've faced?
?
The passage says God created creation not for His profit but for them to profit from Him. Are you actually 'profiting' from your existence by drawing closer to Him, or are you wasting this divine gift? What specific changes would show you are taking this purpose seriously?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that even rescuing a drowning person is good in the intellect without expecting reward. When was the last time you did a good deed solely for God without any desire for praise or return? What holds you back from being that selfless?

Distinction Between Necessary Good and Attributed Good in Divine Actions
The author distinguishes between two ways good/benefit relates to divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between good and benefit being necessary concomitants of divine actions versus being the motive for those actions, and why this distinction matters in debates about divine motivation.

Core Idea
Goodness can accompany an action without being its motive.

This passage addresses a key point in the debate over whether God acts for a purpose. The author affirms that divine actions are good and beneficial, but denies that God acts for the sake of obtaining benefit. To clarify this, he distinguishes between goodness being necessarily attached to an action and goodness being the reason for the action. This distinction is illustrated with an analogy about two types of perfection: one that necessarily accompanies a thing, and one that causes the thing to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once through
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the key terms 'luzum' and 'haml'.
The passage uses a contrast structure, so note the 'but' (بل) and the 'distinction between' (فرق بين).
2
Identify the two kinds of goodness
Find where the author distinguishes between 'luzum al-khayr wa al-salah' (necessity of good and benefit) and 'haml al-khayr wa al-salah' (attribution of good and benefit). Understand what each means in context.
The author says the real debate is about motive, not about whether actions are good.
3
Analyze the analogy with perfection
The author compares this distinction to two types of perfection: one that is necessary for existence (like a necessary attribute) and one that demands existence (like a motivating cause). See how the analogy clarifies the point.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Many mistakenly think that denying that God acts for benefit means denying that His actions are good. The author shows this is false by separating the two meanings of goodness.
This misconception is directly addressed in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتملت على خير وتوجهت إلى صلاح، وأنه لم يخلق الخلق لأجل الفساد، ولكن الكلام إنما وقع في أن الحامل له على الفعل ما كان صلاحًا يرتقبه وخيرا يتوقعه، بل لا حامل له، وفرق بين لزوم الخير والصلاح لأوضاع الأفعال وبين حمل الخير والصلاح على وضع الأفعال، كما يفرق فرقًا ضروريًا بين الكمال الذي يلزم وجود الشيء، وبين الكمال الذي يستدعي وجود الشيء، فإن الأول فضيلة هي كالصفة اللازمة، والثاني فضيلة كالعلة الحاملة.
It comprises good and is directed to benefit, and that He did not create creation for corruption, but the discussion is only about whether the motive for Him to act was benefit that He expects and good that He anticipates, or rather there is no motive for Him. And there is a distinction between the necessity of good and benefit for the positions of actions and the attribution of good and benefit to the position of actions, just as there is an essential distinction between the perfection that accompanies the existence of a thing and the perfection that demands the existence of a thing. For the first is a virtue like a necessary attribute, and the second is a virtue like a motivating cause.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage states a preliminary distinction. The author clarifies that the discussion is not about whether God's actions are good or beneficial in themselves (they are), but about whether God acts for a motive of seeking benefit. He distinguishes between good/benefit being necessarily concomitant with actions (luzum) and being the motive (haml) for those actions. He analogizes this to two types of perfection: one that necessarily accompanies a thing's existence (like a necessary attribute), and one that causes the thing to exist (like a motivating cause). This sets up the debate with the Mu'tazila.

Misconception: Denying that God acts for benefit means God's actions are not good or beneficial.
Correction: The author shows that goodness/benefit can be a necessary consequence of an action without being its motive, so denying motive does not deny inherent goodness.

How often do you measure your spiritual acts by their immediate benefits rather than their intrinsic worth? What would change in your daily practice if you truly internalized that goodness can be its own reason?
?
The lesson uses an analogy: perfection that accompanies existence vs. perfection that demands existence. Consider your own character: are there virtues you possess that are simply part of who you are (like kindness without motive), or do you only exhibit virtues when there is something to gain? Which kind of perfection do you most closely resemble, and what one step can you take today to move toward being good without needing a reason?

The Mu'tazila Argument: Divine Wisdom Necessitates Benefit
Mu'tazila argue divine wisdom necessitates acting for benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's wisdom requires Him to act for the benefit of others, not arbitrarily.

Core Idea
God's wisdom means He never acts arbitrarily; He always intends good for others.

The Mu'tazila base their argument on the agreed attribute of divine wisdom. They define 'wise' in a specific way that leads to the conclusion that God must act for benefit, especially for creation. This passage presents their key reasoning and a scriptural support.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the Mu'tazila argument. Notice the logical progression from premise to conclusion.
The passage is a continuous argument; read it as a whole.
2
Identify the premise and definition
The Mu'tazila start with the fact that God is wise. They define 'wise' as one whose actions are precise and perfect. Ask why this definition is important for their argument.
They derive that a wise being does not act arbitrarily.
3
Trace the conclusion about benefit
From the definition, they conclude that God must intend benefit (salah) and desire good (khayr). Note how they classify benefit into two types and argue that since God is self-sufficient, the benefit must be for others.
The example of rescuing drowning illustrates rationally good action without self-interest.
4
Reflect on the scriptural support
They cite a scriptural saying that God created creation not for profit but for them to profit from Him. Consider how this supports their claim that God acts for the benefit of others.
This shows the argument is not merely rational but also grounded in revealed texts.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
Review the common misunderstanding: the Mu'tazila do not claim God is needy; rather, they argue that wisdom necessitates purposeful action for others. Recognize why this distinction matters.
The author will later respond to this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: قد قام الدليل على أن الرب تعالى حكيم، والحكيم من تكون أفعاله على إحكام وإتقان، فلا يفعل فعلا جزافًا، فإن وقع خيراً فخير، وإن وقع شرا فشر، بل لا بد وأن ينجو غرضا ويقصد صلاحًا ويريد خيرا، ثم إن النفع ينقسم إلى ما يرجع إلى الفاعل إن كان محتاجًا إليه أو إلى غيره، وإن كان الفاعل غنيًّا غير محتاج كإنقاذ الغرقى وتخليص الهلكي مستحسن في العقل، وربما لا يكون المنقذ مكتسبا نفعًا ومتوقعاً حمدًا أو أجرًا، وعن ذلك ورد في بعض الكتب ما خلقت الخلق لا ربح عليهم بل خلقتهم ليربحوا علي.
The Mu'tazila said: The proof has been established that the Lord, exalted, is wise. The wise person is one whose actions are precise and perfect; so he does not act arbitrarily. If it happens to be good, it is good; if it happens to be evil, it is evil. Rather, he must attain an objective, intend benefit, and desire good. Then benefit is divided into that which returns to the agent if he is in need of it, or to others. If the agent is self-sufficient without need, like rescuing the drowning and saving the perishing, this is considered good in reason, and perhaps the rescuer does not acquire benefit nor expect praise or reward. About that it is related in some scriptures: 'I created creation not for profit from them, but I created them that they might profit from Me.'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
The Mu'tazila present their case. They begin from the agreed premise that God is wise, and define the wise as one whose actions are precise and flawless. Therefore, they argue, God does not act arbitrarily (juzaf); rather, His actions have purposes: He intends benefit (salah) and desires good (khayr). They then classify benefit as either returning to the agent (if the agent is needy) or to others. Since God is self-sufficient, the benefit He aims for cannot be for Himself; it must be for others. They give the example of rescuing a drowning person, an act considered rationally good even if the rescuer expects no reward. They cite a scriptural saying: 'I created creation not for profit from them, but I created them that they might profit from Me.' This sets the stage for the author's response.

إحكام وإتقان
جزاف
صلاح
خير
نفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila argue that God acts for His own benefit or that He is needy.
Correction: They explicitly say benefit returns to the agent only if the agent is in need; God is self-sufficient, so the benefit is for others.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila definition of 'wise'?
A) One whose actions are good
B) One whose actions are precise and perfect
C) One who acts only for His own benefit
D) One who creates without purpose

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that God's wisdom, defined as precision and perfection, requires Him to act for the good of creation, not for His own benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila argue that God created you not for His profit but for you to profit from Him. What does 'profiting from God' mean in your daily life? Are you living as though He needs something from you, or are you actively seeking to receive from Him? Commit to one concrete act today that seeks His provision rather than trying to give Him something.
?
The passage defines a wise person as one who acts with precision and perfection, not arbitrarily. Look at your own decisions this week. How many were made impulsively or without clear purpose? In what area of your life can you begin to imitate this attribute of God by acting with intentionality and care, seeking benefit for others?

Wisdom as Perfection of Self-Sufficiency
Mu'tazila argue that wisdom perfects self-sufficiency, not negates it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that affirming divine wisdom in creation does not imply need; rather, it demonstrates the perfection of God's self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Wisdom in creation does not imply need; it highlights God's perfect independence.

The Mu'tazila anticipate an objection: if God acts wisely for a purpose, doesn't that suggest need? In this passage, they turn the argument around, claiming that the very dependence of all creation on God is what reveals His perfect self-sufficiency. Let's examine how they make this case.

Guided Path
1
Read with the objection in mind
The Mu'tazila respond to the charge that purpose implies need. Notice their opening statement: wisdom does not negate self-sufficiency, but perfects it.
2
Identify key terms
Look for ghina (self-sufficiency), kamal al-ghina (perfection of self-sufficiency), ihtiyaj (need), and infiradi bi-ghinahu (His exclusivity in self-sufficiency).
These terms form the conceptual framework.
3
Trace the logical steps
First, they assert that wisdom does not negate self-sufficiency but perfects it. Then they explain how self-sufficiency is known through the world's need. Finally, they point to signs of wisdom in creation.
The argument moves from assertion to explanation to evidence.
4
Consider the implication
How does the argument defend God's transcendence while preserving the reality of divine purposes? It turns a potential objection into a proof of perfection.
5
Reflect on the closing remark
The passage says only 'corrupt imaginations' would reject these signs. What does that suggest about sound reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وما ذكرناه لا ينافي الغنى، بل هو كمال الغنى عن خلقه، فإن كمال الغنى لا يعرف إلا باحتياج كل العالم إليه واحتياج العالم إنما يعرفه العالم. فيستدل به على انفراده تعالى بغناه، والله تعالى في كل صنع من صنائعه حكمة ظاهرة، وآية تدل على وحدانيته باهرة، لا تنكرها العقول السليمة، ولا ينبو عنها إلا الأوهام السقيمة.
They said: What we have mentioned does not negate self-sufficiency; rather, it is the perfection of self-sufficiency from His creation. For the perfection of self-sufficiency is only known by the need of all the world for Him, and the world's need is only known by the rational being. So it is inferred from that His exclusivity, exalted, in His self-sufficiency. And God, exalted, in every act of His acts has apparent wisdom and a manifest sign indicating His oneness, which sound intellects do not deny, and only corrupt imaginations recoil from.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
The Mu'tazila respond to a potential objection: that attributing purpose to God's actions might imply need. They assert that the opposite is true: recognizing that all creation depends on God for benefit highlights His perfect independence. The need of the world for Him is what makes His self-sufficiency known. Moreover, every act of God displays wisdom and signs of His oneness, which sound minds readily affirm. Only diseased imaginations would deny this.

Key Terms
الغنى
كمال الغنى
احتياج
انفراده بالغنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing purposes to God's actions implies that He needs something outside Himself.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that recognizing the world's dependency on God actually highlights His perfect self-sufficiency, not need.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, how does divine wisdom relate to God's self-sufficiency?
A) It contradicts it
B) It perfects it
C) It is unrelated
D) It implies need

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defend divine wisdom as perfecting self-sufficiency, not diminishing it, and argue that creation's dependence reveals God's exclusive independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you look at the world around you, do you see evidence of divine wisdom that points to God's exclusive self-sufficiency? How can you actively train your mind to see these signs and strengthen your conviction in Tawheed?
?
If you truly believed that God's wisdom does not imply any need on His part, how would it change the way you pray—would you still pray out of a sense of obligation, or out of gratitude that your dependence reveals His perfection? What would it look like to make that shift today?

Ahl al-Haqq Redefine Wisdom as Acting According to Knowledge
Ahl al-Haqq respond to the Mu'tazilite objection by redefining wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq's definition of divine wisdom as actions occurring according to God's eternal knowledge, and how this avoids attributing motives of benefit to Allah.

Core Idea
Wisdom is not about benefit; it is about perfect conformity to God's knowledge.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that a wise being must act for a purpose or benefit. Here, Ahl al-Haqq respond by accepting that wisdom implies precision, but they shift the meaning: precision means alignment with divine knowledge, not pursuit of benefit. This short passage establishes a key Ash'ari principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice how the argument unfolds: concession, redefinition, conclusion.
The Arabic is compact; pause after each clause.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'حكيم', 'محكمة متقنة', 'حسب علمه', 'جزاف', 'اتفاق'. How do they shape the argument?
These terms are central to the Ash'ari position.
3
Compare interpretations of precision
Contrast the Mu'tazilite view (precision implies benefit) with the Ahl al-Haqq view (precision implies conformity to knowledge). Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for divine transcendence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق: مسلّم أن الحكيم من كانت أفعاله محكمة متقنة، أو إنما تكون محكمة إذا وقعت على حسب علمه، وإذا حصلت على حسب علمه لم تكن جزافًا ولا وقعت بالاتفاق.
The people of truth said: It is conceded that the wise person is one whose actions are precise and perfect, or rather they are only precise when they occur according to His knowledge. And if they occur according to His knowledge, they are not arbitrary nor do they happen by chance.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox Sunni position) accept the premise that wisdom implies precise and perfect actions, but they interpret 'precision' differently. For them, an action is precise not because it aims at some benefit external to the agent, but because it conforms to God's eternal knowledge. Since God's knowledge is perfect, whatever He creates is precisely as it should be according to His knowledge, and is therefore not arbitrary (juzaf) or accidental (ittifaq). This definition avoids attributing a motive of benefit to God while preserving the perfection of His actions.

Key Terms
حكيم
محكمة متقنة
حسب علمه
جزاف
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wisdom requires that God's actions aim at some benefit or purpose external to Him.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq argue that wisdom means actions are precise according to God's knowledge, not that they are beneficial. This preserves God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what makes an action wise?
A) It benefits others
B) It occurs according to God's knowledge
C) It is arbitrary
D) It is beneficial to the agent

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq define wisdom as conformity to divine knowledge, thereby avoiding arbitrariness without attributing benefit to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you aim for them to be 'precise and perfect' according to God's knowledge, or do you often act arbitrarily based on your own desires? What would change if you truly believed that wisdom means conformity to divine knowledge?
?
If God's wisdom is not defined by benefit but by perfect alignment with His knowledge, how does that challenge your understanding of why you face trials in your life? Can you accept that even when you see no benefit, His actions are still wise?
?
This lesson redefines wisdom away from human notions of benefit. Are you willing to trust that God's actions are wise even when they bring you pain or loss? What concrete step can you take this week to live out that trust?

Objection and Answer on Divine Wisdom
The author reports an objection about self-sufficiency and answers it with a definition of wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of wisdom (ḥikma) resolves the apparent tension between divine self-sufficiency and purposeful creation.

Core Idea
True wisdom is acting according to knowledge, not needing anything from creation.

This lesson presents an objection: the order and purpose in creation might seem to imply that God needs something from it, which would contradict His absolute self-sufficiency. The orthodox response (from 'ahl al-ḥaqq') clarifies that wisdom means actions are precise and based on knowledge, not that they fulfill a need. Understanding this distinction safeguards God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the opponents state that their earlier claim (about creation having purpose) does not negate God's self-sufficiency; rather it demonstrates it.
Notice how they argue that the world's need for God proves His independence.
2
Identify the opponents' core claim
The opponents assert that every act of God contains manifest wisdom and signs of oneness that sound intellects accept. They think this supports their view.
Their claim seems to align with Islamic belief, but the response will refine the meaning of 'wisdom'.
3
Examine the response's definition
The people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq) concede the point but define 'wise' as one whose actions are precise (muḥkama mutqana) — meaning they occur according to His knowledge. This eliminates randomness or chance.
This redefinition shows that wisdom is about knowledge and precision, not about benefit to the agent.
4
Connect to self-sufficiency
Reflect on how this definition resolves the objection: God's actions are wise because they are perfectly ordered according to His knowledge, not because He benefits. Thus His self-sufficiency remains intact.
The key is that wisdom does not imply need; it implies perfect knowledge and execution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وما ذكرناه لا ينافي الغنى، بل هو كمال الغنى عن خلقه، فإن كمال الغنى لا يعرف إلا باحتياج كل العالم إليه واحتياج العالم إنما يعرفه العالم. فيستدل به على انفراده تعالى بغناه، والله تعالى في كل صنع من صنائعه حكمة ظاهرة، وآية تدل على وحدانيته باهرة، لا تنكرها العقول السليمة، ولا ينبو عنها إلا الأوهام السقيمة. قال أهل الحق: مسلّم أن الحكيم من كانت أفعاله محكمة متقنة، أو إنما تكون محكمة إذا وقعت على حسب علمه، وإذا حصلت على حسب علمه لم تكن جزافًا ولا وقعت بالاتفاق.
They said: What we have mentioned does not negate self-sufficiency; rather it is the perfection of self-sufficiency from His creation. For the perfection of self-sufficiency is only known through the need of all the world for Him, and the world's need is only known by the world. So it is used as evidence for His exclusive self-sufficiency. And God Almighty has in every one of His works a manifest wisdom, and a dazzling sign indicating His oneness, which sound intellects do not deny and only sick fancies recoil from. The people of truth said: It is conceded that the wise one is he whose actions are precise and perfect, or rather they are only precise when they occur according to His knowledge, and when they occur according to His knowledge they are not haphazard and do not happen by chance.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage records an objection raised by opponents and the response of the orthodox (ahl al-ḥaqq). The opponents claim that their earlier statement about creation having a purpose is not incompatible with God's absolute self-sufficiency (ghinā); rather, it actually demonstrates His perfect self-sufficiency, because the creation's need for Him proves His independence. Moreover, every act of God contains manifest wisdom and signs of oneness that sane minds accept. The response from the people of truth accepts that but clarifies the definition of 'wise' (ḥakīm): a wise agent is one whose actions are precise and perfect (muḥkama mutqana). Precision means the act occurs according to the agent's knowledge. If it occurs according to His knowledge, it cannot be random or accidental. Thus the objectors' point is conceded but redefined in a way that maintains God's wisdom without implying need or benefit for Himself.

Key Terms
الغنى
الحكمة
محكمة متقنة
على حسب علمه
جزافا
بالاتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if creation has purpose and wisdom, then God must need something from creation, which would contradict His self-sufficiency.
Correction: The passage shows that wisdom is defined as acting according to knowledge, not as fulfilling a need. Thus creation's order actually demonstrates God's perfect self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the people of truth, what makes an action 'precise' (muḥkama)?
A) It benefits the doer
B) It occurs according to the doer's knowledge
C) It happens by chance
D) It is done for a specific end

Key Takeaway
The definition of wisdom as acting according to knowledge resolves the objection that creation implies need in God, preserving His absolute self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you assume that God's wisdom means He gives you what you need, rather than acting according to perfect knowledge? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
When you face events that seem random or haphazard, do you believe that they are still occurring according to God's knowledge? Are you willing to trust His wisdom even when you cannot see the precision?
?
How does defining wisdom as acting according to knowledge, not benefit, change how you view your own decisions? Are your actions based on knowledge of truth or on what benefits you? Ask Allah to make you wise.

Obligation Undermines Gratitude
Ash'ari objection that obligation negates gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris argue that if God is obligated to do what is sound, He would not deserve thanks or praise, and the implications of this for the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
An obligation fulfilled deserves no extra praise.

The Ash'ari objection presented here targets the very concept of divine gratitude. If God were compelled to do what is best, then His actions would be mere fulfillment of a debt, not acts of grace. This lesson unpacks that argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure and the analogy at the end.
The key term is 'وجوب' (obligation).
2
Identify the core argument
The objection states: if every good action of God is obligatory, then doing it does not merit thanks or praise. The same applies to a servant who obeys under obligation.
The analogy of paying a debt is crucial.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy: paying a debt does not entitle one to additional reward. How does this apply to God's actions?
4
Connect to the broader debate
Think about why this objection undermines the Mu'tazila view that God must do the most sound. If that were true, God would lose His praiseworthiness.
This shows the Ash'ari emphasis on God's absolute freedom and grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن كل ما فعله الرب تعالى من الصلاح لو كان حتما عليه لما استوجب بفعل ما شكرا أو حمدا، فإنه في فعله قضى ما وجب عليه، وما استوجب عبد بطاعته ثوابا وتفضيلا فإنه في طاعته قضى ما وجب عليه، ومن قضى دينه لم يستوجب شيئا آخر.
And among them that if everything that the Lord, exalted, does of soundness were obligatory upon Him, then by doing what He did He would not deserve thanks or praise, for in His action He fulfilled what was obligatory upon Him, and if a servant by his obedience deserves reward and preference, then in his obedience he fulfilled what was obligatory upon him, and one who fulfills his debt does not deserve anything else.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This objection argues that if it were obligatory upon God to do what is sound, then He would not deserve thanks or praise for doing it, because He is merely fulfilling an obligation. Similarly, if a servant obeys because it is obligatory, he would not deserve reward for that obedience, since he is just doing what is required. The analogy is drawn: one who pays a debt does not deserve additional remuneration. Thus, the Mu‘tazila position that God must do the most sound would undermine the very concepts of divine gratitude and meritorious reward.

Key Terms
شكر
حمد
ثواب
تفضل
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that if God does good, He is always worthy of praise regardless of obligation.
Correction: This passage shows that if an action is obligatory, fulfilling it does not merit additional praise, just as paying a debt does not deserve payment.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God's actions are all obligatory?
A) God would be praised even more
B) God would not deserve thanks or praise for those actions
C) Humans would not be obligated to obey
D) Reward would become greater

Key Takeaway
If God were obligated to do good, He would not be praiseworthy for doing so, undermining the concept of divine gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank God for a blessing, do you ever secretly feel that He owes it to you because of your good deeds? How does this lesson reveal the ingratitude hidden in that assumption? Will you today thank Him purely for His generosity, not as a repayment?
?
Think of one act of worship you performed today. Did you do it with a sense of duty or with the joy of a gift? If it was duty, how does this lesson challenge you to transform that act into one of gratitude?
?
If you were to view all of God's blessings as obligations He must fulfill, would your heart still be filled with praise? This lesson says no. Have you ever felt your worship become transactional? What step will you take this week to break free from that mindset?
