Four Conditions for God Leading Astray
Answer stating four conditions for the permissibility of God leading astray.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conditions under which Islamic theologians permit the attribution of leading astray (iḍlāl) to God, and why these conditions are necessary to preserve divine knowledge and truthfulness.

Core Idea
God's leading astray is restricted by His own knowledge and truthfulness.

This lesson presents a nuanced theological answer to the objection that God cannot lead people astray. The author permits iḍlāl, but only under four precise conditions that safeguard God's omniscience and veracity. The passage then illustrates the first two conditions with concrete examples involving messengers and miracles, showing the logical constraints on divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the four conditions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Look for the four conditions the author lists for God's leading astray to be permissible. These are introduced after 'but on condition that'.
The conditions are: (1) not contradicting what is known, (2) no contradiction between evidence and evidenced, (3) not leading to incapacitation, (4) not leading to falsehood.
2
Understand the first condition with its example
Focus on the author's explanation of the first condition: if God knows He will send a guide, He cannot lead people astray by that same evidence, as it would contradict His knowledge.
The example shows the necessity of consistency between God's knowledge and His actions.
3
Understand the second condition with its example
Now examine the second condition: no contradiction between evidence and the thing evidenced. The author explains that if God informs about a messenger's truthfulness, He cannot then falsify that messenger.
This preserves divine truthfulness and logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الرابع نقول : نحن نجوز الإضلال على الله تعالى، ولكن بشرط أن لا يقع خلاف المعلوم، وبشرط أن لا يتناقض الدليل والمقدول ولا يلتبس الدليل والشبهة، وبشرط أن لا يؤدي الأمر إلى التعجيز، وبشرط أن لا يؤدي إلى التكذيب في القول، ونذكر لكل واحد وجها ومثالاً ; فنقول : إذا علم الرب تعالى أنه يرسل رسولا يهتدي به قوم فهو كما يعلم أنه ينصب دليلا يستدل به قوم، فلو أضلهم بعين ذلك الدليل وقع الأمر على خلاف المعلوم وذلك محال. وكذلك إذا أخبر أنه يرسل رسولا يهتدي به ثم أضل كل من بعث إليه تناقض الخبر وانقلب الصدق كذبا وذلك محال، فإن الكذب لا يجوز على الله تعالى، وإنما لا يجوز ذلك عليه ; لأن الكذب إخبار عن الشيء على خلاف ما هو به، وهو يعلمه على ما هو به وكل من علم شيئًا كان له خبر عن معلومه، والخبر عن المعلوم خبر عن ما هو به، فلا يجتمع في العالم خبران متناقضان، وإذا علم الرب تعالى صدق النبي وأخبر عن صدقه لفقد صدقه ومن صدقه فلا يجوز أن يكذبه، ومن وجه تناقض الدليل أن جهة الدلالة في القرينة وفي الفعل لا تختلف، فإذا دل الشيء على شيء لا يجوز أن يدل على خلافه، فإن ذلك غير مقدور. كما أن جهة التخصيص إذا دلت على الإرادة لم تدل على خلاف ذلك، فجهة التخصيص بالتصديق لا تدل على خلاف قصد المخصص بالتصديق، فإرسال رسول وإخلاؤه عن دليل الصدق وإظهار معجزة والقصد بها إلى إضلال الخلق وإظهار خارق للعادة على يدي كاذب في معارضة دعوى النبي كل ذلك
As for the answer to the fourth question, we say: We permit leading astray (iḍlāl) from God Almighty, but on condition that it does not occur contrary to what is known, and on condition that the evidence and the thing evidenced do not contradict each other and that the evidence and the doubt are not confused, and on condition that the matter does not lead to incapacitation, and on condition that it does not lead to falsification in speech. And we will mention for each one an aspect and an example. So we say: When the Lord Almighty knows that He will send a messenger by whom a people will be guided, just as He knows that He will set up an evidence by which a people will be led, then if He were to lead them astray by that same evidence, the matter would occur contrary to what is known, and that is impossible. Likewise, if He informs that He will send a messenger by whom people will be guided, then He leads everyone to whom he was sent astray, the information would contradict itself and truth would turn into falsehood, and that is impossible. For falsehood is not permissible for God Almighty; it is only impermissible for Him because falsehood is informing about something contrary to what it is, while He knows it as it is, and everyone who knows something has a report about his known object, and the report about the known object is a report about what it is, so two contradictory reports cannot coexist in the universe. And when the Lord Almighty knows the truthfulness of the prophet and informs about his truthfulness, due to his lack of truthfulness and from his truthfulness, He is not allowed to falsify him. And from one aspect, the contradiction of the evidence is that the direction of indication in the context and in the act does not differ; so if a thing indicates a thing, it is not permissible that it indicates its opposite, for that is not possible. Just as the direction of specification, when it indicates intent, does not indicate the opposite of that, so the direction of specification by affirmation does not indicate contrary to the intention of the specifier by affirmation. So sending a messenger and leaving him devoid of evidence of truthfulness, or showing a miracle with the intent to mislead the creation, or showing a miracle contrary to nature at the hand of a liar in opposition to the prophet's claim—all of that is [impossible].
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the fourth objection concerning God leading people astray (iḍlāl). The author first permits that God may lead astray, but only under four conditions: (1) that it does not contradict what God knows (al-ma'lūm), (2) that it does not cause contradiction between the evidence (dalīl) and the thing evidenced (madlūl), nor confusion between evidence and doubt (shubhah), (3) that it does not lead to incapacity (ta'jīz), and (4) that it does not lead to falsehood in speech (takdhīb). Then he gives examples. For the first condition: if God knows that He will send a messenger by whom a people will be guided, just as He knows that He will establish an evidence by which people will be guided, then if He were to lead them astray by that same evidence, it would contradict His knowledge—this is impossible. For the second condition: if He informs that He will send a messenger by whom people will be guided, then He leads them all astray, the information would contradict itself, and truth would become falsehood; falsehood is not permissible for God because it is informing contrary to reality while He knows the reality. Furthermore, the direction of indication (jihah al-dalālah) in the context and the action does not vary, so if something indicates a thing, it cannot indicate its opposite—that is not possible. Similarly, the direction of specification (jihah al-takhṣīṣ) when it indicates intent does not indicate the opposite. Therefore, sending a messenger without evidence of truthfulness, or showing a miracle with the intent to mislead people, or showing a miracle on the hand of a liar to oppose the prophet's claim—all of that is impossible.

المعلوم
الدليل
المدلول
التناقض
التعجيز
التكذيب
جهة الدلالة
جهة التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that God's own knowledge and truthfulness impose limits: leading astray must not contradict divine knowledge, cause logical contradictions, incapacitate, or result in falsehood.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Impossibility of Contradicting God's Truthful Report
Proof that God's truthful report precludes subsequent misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's truthful report cannot be contradicted by subsequent actions, based on the impossibility of lying for God.

Core Idea
God's promise and His act cannot contradict because His knowledge never changes.

This lesson continues the theme of divine impossibility. The author builds a second proof based on God's truthful report. If God announces He will send a guiding prophet, then misleading the people would contradict that report. The argument hinges on the nature of lying and God's perfect knowledge. Let's examine how the author establishes the impossibility of such contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the logical flow: hypothesis, consequence, reason, conclusion.
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
What does God report, and what hypothetical action follows? Notice the contradiction that arises.
The scenario imagines God sending a guide then misleading the people.
3
Understand why lying is impossible for God
The author defines lying as reporting contrary to reality, and states that God knows reality as it is. Grasp why divine knowledge prevents lying.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Why cannot two contradictory reports exist? How does this impossibility reinforce the necessity of God's truthfulness?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا أخبر أنه يرسل رسولا يهتدي به ثم أضل كل من بعث إليه تناقض الخبر وانقلب الصدق كذبا وذلك محال، فإن الكذب لا يجوز على الله تعالى، وإنما لا يجوز ذلك عليه؛ لأن الكذب إخبار عن الشيء على خلاف ما هو به، وهو يعلمه على ما هو به وكل من علم شيئا كان له خبر عن معلومه، والخبر عن المعلوم خبر عن ما هو به، فلا يجتمع في العالم خبران متناقضان.
And likewise, if He reports that He will send a messenger by whom [people] will be guided, then He misleads every one to whom he was sent, the report would be contradictory and truth would turn into falsehood, and that is impossible. For lying is not permissible for God, exalted, and it is not permissible for Him only because lying is reporting about a thing contrary to what it is, while He knows it as it is; and whoever knows something, his report about what he knows is a report about it as it is. So two contradictory reports cannot coexist in the world.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a second proof, this time based on God's truthful report. The author argues that if God announces He will send a prophet who will guide people, then subsequently misleads everyone to whom that prophet was sent, God's own report would become contradictory, turning truth into falsehood. Lying (kadhib) is impossible for God because it involves reporting something contrary to reality, whereas God knows reality as it is. A report based on knowledge must correspond to the known reality. Therefore, two contradictory reports (one stating guidance, the other resulting in misguidance) cannot coexist. Thus, such an act is impossible.

First Objection: Ontological Status of the Quran
First objection: dilemma over Quran's eternal or human nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed by opponents: if the Quran is eternal it cannot be a miracle, and if it is a human action it also cannot be a miracle.

Core Idea
The Quran's eternal nature and human acquisition both seem to disqualify it from being a miracle.

Opponents of the doctrine of i‘jaz al-Quran argue that the Quran cannot be a miracle whether it is considered as an eternal attribute of God or as a human recitation. They challenge its supporters to explain how it can be miraculous if it is either uncreated or created through human power. Let's examine the first objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first objection
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection and its translation. Note how the opponents frame the dilemma.
The objection uses a dichotomy: either eternal attribute or human action, both problematic.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two alternatives: (1) the Quran as eternal attribute → cannot be temporal miracle; (2) the Quran as human action → cannot be beyond human power.
3
Analyze the rebuttal attempt
The opponents anticipate a response (God creates it without acquisition) and then counter by asking about the locus of creation. Understand the counter-argument.
If created in the prophet's tongue, it's within his power; if elsewhere, the miracle is unheard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الزيغ والباطل : لنا على مساق ما ذكرتموه من إعجاز القرآن سؤالان؛ أحدهما أن القرآن بمعنى المقروء والمكتوب صفة قديمة عندكم، والقديم لا يكون معجزا، وبمعنى القراءة هو فعل القارئ وتلاوته، وفعل العبد لا يكون معجزا. فإن قلتم : إن الرب تعالى يخلقه في الحال من غير كسب النبي ، قيل : وفي أي محل يخلقه أفي لسانه ومن المعلوم أن الحرف والصوت القائم بلسانه ومخارج حروفه مقدورة له والمعجزة لا تكون مقدورة للعبد، أم في محل آخر من شجرة أو لوح أو قلب ملك؟ فالمعجزة ذلك المخلوق لا ما نطق به النبي، فما هو معجزة لم نسمعه وما سمعناه ليس بمعجزة فما الجواب عن هذا السؤال؟ والثاني : أنكم قلتم : وجه إعجاز القرآن فصاحته وجزالته ونظمه وبلاغته، فما حدود هذه المعاني؟ أولا حتى نبين حقائقها فنتكلم عليها، أهي بإفرادها معجزة أم بمجموعها ؟ وقد رأينا كم اختلفتم في أن القرآن معجزة من جهة صرف الدواعي، أم من جهة ما اشتمل عليه من بديع النظم والفصاحة، ومن قال بالقسم الثاني اختلف في أن الفصاحة بديعة أم الجزالة أم النظم، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن المعجز يجب أن يكون ظاهراً لكل من هو في حقه معجز، ظهورا لا يستراب فيه البتة ، ومن قال منكم : إنه معجز من حيث الفصاحة فقط جوز أن يكون في كلام العرب مثله من حيث النظم والجزالة ومن قال بالثاني فقد جوز المماثلة في الأول فلم يظهر ظهور انقلاب الجماد حيوانا والبحر يبسا والحجر الصلد عينا نضاخة، إلى غير ذلك من معجزات الأنبياء عليهم السلام، إذ ظهرت على قضية لم يبق للتردد فيها مجال.
The people of deviation and falsehood said: We have two objections regarding the course of what you have mentioned of the inimitability of the Quran: First, that the Quran in the sense of the recited and written is an eternal attribute with you, and the eternal cannot be a miracle; and in the sense of recitation it is the action of the reciter and his reading, and the action of the servant cannot be a miracle. If you say: The Lord, exalted, creates it instantaneously without the prophet's acquisition, it is said: And in which locus does He create it? In his tongue? And it is known that the letter and sound subsisting in his tongue and the points of articulation of his letters are within his power, and a miracle cannot be within the power of the servant; or in another locus, such as a tree, a tablet, or the heart of an angel? Then the miracle is that created thing, not what the prophet uttered; so what is a miracle we have not heard, and what we have heard is not a miracle. So what is the answer to this objection? Second: You said that the aspect of the Quran's inimitability is its eloquence (fasahah), stylistic excellence (jazalah), composition (nazm), and rhetorical beauty (balaghah). So what are the definitions of these meanings? First, until we clarify their realities and discuss them, are they individually miraculous or collectively? And we have seen how much you have disagreed: is the Quran a miracle from the aspect of the prevention of aspirations (sarf al-dawaa‘i), or from the aspect of what it contains of wonderful composition and eloquence? And whoever said the second group disagreed: is the eloquence wonderful, or the stylistic excellence, or the composition? And it is known without doubt that a miracle must be apparent to everyone for whom it is a miracle, an appearance with absolutely no suspicion. And whoever among you said that it is miraculous only from the aspect of eloquence allowed that there could be something like it in the speech of the Arabs from the aspect of composition and stylistic excellence; and whoever said the second allowed similarity in the first. So it did not become apparent like the transformation of an inanimate object into an animal, or the sea into dry land, or the hard rock into a gushing spring, and so on of the miracles of the prophets, peace be upon them, when they appeared in a manner that left no room for doubt.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by opponents against the doctrine of the Quran's inimitability (i‘jaz). The first objection focuses on the ontological status of the Quran: if it is eternal (qadim) as an attribute of God, it cannot be a miracle because miracles are temporal events; if it is human action (fi‘l al-‘abd), it cannot be a miracle because human actions are not miraculous. The opponents then challenge the possible loci of creation: if God creates the Quran in the tongue of the prophet, it would be within the prophet's power (maqdur), which contradicts the nature of a miracle; if in another locus, then the miracle is that created thing which is unheard, while what is heard is not a miracle. The second objection concerns the definition of the miraculous aspect: the opponents note that scholars disagree on whether the miracle lies in the prevention of aspirations (sarf al-dawaa‘i) or in the linguistic excellence of composition and eloquence, and even within the latter, there is disagreement on which specific quality (fasahah, jazalah, nazm) is the miracle. They argue that a true miracle must be manifestly clear to all, but these disagreements show lack of clarity, unlike the obvious physical miracles of earlier prophets.

Key Terms
إعجاز القرآن
معجزة
صفة قديمة
فعل العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that the Quran cannot be a miracle whether it is considered eternal or a human action, and attempts to resolve by divine creation still fail.

Second Objection: Defining the Miraculous Aspect
Second objection: disagreement on the miraculous aspect undermines clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection: the lack of agreement on what exactly makes the Quran miraculous undermines its claim to be a clear miracle like those of previous prophets.

Core Idea
Disagreement among scholars on the miraculous aspect shows the Quran's inimitability is not as clear as physical miracles.

The second objection attacks the lack of consensus on the precise aspect of the Quran's inimitability. The opponents point out that Muslim scholars disagree whether the miracle lies in prevention of aspirations (sarf al-dawaa‘i) or in linguistic excellence, and even within the latter, which feature is miraculous. This ambiguity contradicts the requirement that a miracle be manifestly clear to all.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Notice how the opponents list the areas of disagreement.
The objection hinges on the lack of clarity and consensus.
2
Identify the two main positions on i‘jaz
Identify the two broad views: sarf al-dawaa‘i (prevention of challenging) versus linguistic excellence (fasahah, jazalah, nazm).
Then note subdivisions within the linguistic excellence camp.
3
Understand the requirement for a miracle
The opponents assert that a miracle must be 'apparent to everyone... with absolutely no suspicion.' Compare this to the clear physical miracles of past prophets.
The disagreement among scholars shows lack of such clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الزيغ والباطل : لنا على مساق ما ذكرتموه من إعجاز القرآن سؤالان؛ أحدهما أن القرآن بمعنى المقروء والمكتوب صفة قديمة عندكم، والقديم لا يكون معجزا، وبمعنى القراءة هو فعل القارئ وتلاوته، وفعل العبد لا يكون معجزا. فإن قلتم : إن الرب تعالى يخلقه في الحال من غير كسب النبي ، قيل : وفي أي محل يخلقه أفي لسانه ومن المعلوم أن الحرف والصوت القائم بلسانه ومخارج حروفه مقدورة له والمعجزة لا تكون مقدورة للعبد، أم في محل آخر من شجرة أو لوح أو قلب ملك؟ فالمعجزة ذلك المخلوق لا ما نطق به النبي، فما هو معجزة لم نسمعه وما سمعناه ليس بمعجزة فما الجواب عن هذا السؤال؟ والثاني : أنكم قلتم : وجه إعجاز القرآن فصاحته وجزالته ونظمه وبلاغته، فما حدود هذه المعاني؟ أولا حتى نبين حقائقها فنتكلم عليها، أهي بإفرادها معجزة أم بمجموعها ؟ وقد رأينا كم اختلفتم في أن القرآن معجزة من جهة صرف الدواعي، أم من جهة ما اشتمل عليه من بديع النظم والفصاحة، ومن قال بالقسم الثاني اختلف في أن الفصاحة بديعة أم الجزالة أم النظم، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن المعجز يجب أن يكون ظاهراً لكل من هو في حقه معجز، ظهورا لا يستراب فيه البتة ، ومن قال منكم : إنه معجز من حيث الفصاحة فقط جوز أن يكون في كلام العرب مثله من حيث النظم والجزالة ومن قال بالثاني فقد جوز المماثلة في الأول فلم يظهر ظهور انقلاب الجماد حيوانا والبحر يبسا والحجر الصلد عينا نضاخة، إلى غير ذلك من معجزات الأنبياء عليهم السلام، إذ ظهرت على قضية لم يبق للتردد فيها مجال.
The people of deviation and falsehood said: We have two objections regarding the course of what you have mentioned of the inimitability of the Quran: First, that the Quran in the sense of the recited and written is an eternal attribute with you, and the eternal cannot be a miracle; and in the sense of recitation it is the action of the reciter and his reading, and the action of the servant cannot be a miracle. If you say: The Lord, exalted, creates it instantaneously without the prophet's acquisition, it is said: And in which locus does He create it? In his tongue? And it is known that the letter and sound subsisting in his tongue and the points of articulation of his letters are within his power, and a miracle cannot be within the power of the servant; or in another locus, such as a tree, a tablet, or the heart of an angel? Then the miracle is that created thing, not what the prophet uttered; so what is a miracle we have not heard, and what we have heard is not a miracle. So what is the answer to this objection? Second: You said that the aspect of the Quran's inimitability is its eloquence (fasahah), stylistic excellence (jazalah), composition (nazm), and rhetorical beauty (balaghah). So what are the definitions of these meanings? First, until we clarify their realities and discuss them, are they individually miraculous or collectively? And we have seen how much you have disagreed: is the Quran a miracle from the aspect of the prevention of aspirations (sarf al-dawaa‘i), or from the aspect of what it contains of wonderful composition and eloquence? And whoever said the second group disagreed: is the eloquence wonderful, or the stylistic excellence, or the composition? And it is known without doubt that a miracle must be apparent to everyone for whom it is a miracle, an appearance with absolutely no suspicion. And whoever among you said that it is miraculous only from the aspect of eloquence allowed that there could be something like it in the speech of the Arabs from the aspect of composition and stylistic excellence; and whoever said the second allowed similarity in the first. So it did not become apparent like the transformation of an inanimate object into an animal, or the sea into dry land, or the hard rock into a gushing spring, and so on of the miracles of the prophets, peace be upon them, when they appeared in a manner that left no room for doubt.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by opponents against the doctrine of the Quran's inimitability (i‘jaz). The first objection focuses on the ontological status of the Quran: if it is eternal (qadim) as an attribute of God, it cannot be a miracle because miracles are temporal events; if it is human action (fi‘l al-‘abd), it cannot be a miracle because human actions are not miraculous. The opponents then challenge the possible loci of creation: if God creates the Quran in the tongue of the prophet, it would be within the prophet's power (maqdur), which contradicts the nature of a miracle; if in another locus, then the miracle is that created thing which is unheard, while what is heard is not a miracle. The second objection concerns the definition of the miraculous aspect: the opponents note that scholars disagree on whether the miracle lies in the prevention of aspirations (sarf al-dawaa‘i) or in the linguistic excellence of composition and eloquence, and even within the latter, there is disagreement on which specific quality (fasahah, jazalah, nazm) is the miracle. They argue that a true miracle must be manifestly clear to all, but these disagreements show lack of clarity, unlike the obvious physical miracles of earlier prophets.

Key Terms
فصاحة
جزالة
نظم
بلاغة
صرف الدواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be beyond human power.
B) It must be apparent to everyone without any suspicion.
C) It must involve physical transformation.
D) It must be unique in its eloquence.

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that the lack of consensus on the Quran's miraculous aspect undermines its claim to be a clear miracle like those of earlier prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you encounter disagreements among scholars about matters of faith, do you tend to feel shaken in your certainty, or do you seek to understand the underlying principles? In this lesson, the opponent uses disagreement to cast doubt—do you ever let similar arguments plant seeds of doubt in your own heart? What will you do from now on when faced with such doubts?

The passage describes physical miracles that were immediately clear to everyone. The Quran demands deeper reflection. Are you willing to put in the effort to recognize its miraculous nature, or do you demand instant clarity like the opponents? Be honest: have you ever neglected studying the Quran's linguistic beauty because it required patience?
?
Your belief in the Quran as a miracle rests on rational arguments that not everyone accepts. Does that make your faith weaker or stronger? If someone challenges you using the same objection as this passage—pointing to scholarly disagreement—will you be prepared to respond? What specific step will you take this week to solidify your understanding of the Quran's inimitability?

The Quran's Miracle: Three Modes of Manifestation
Ahl al-Haqq propose three ways the Quran can be a miracle despite being eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Haqq reconcile the eternity of the Quran with its miraculous nature by identifying three distinct ways the Quran can function as a miracle in the created world.

Core Idea
The eternal Quran becomes a miracle through its created, time-bound manifestations.

This lesson tackles a key objection: if the Quran is eternal, how can it be a miracle? The response affirms that miracles belong to temporal events, not the eternal essence. It then explains three ways the Quran can be miraculous: through direct creation of speech in the reciter's tongue (without his power), through inner speech in the prophet's soul (which he then utters), or through angelic mediation (where the angel reveals the speech to the prophet).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Identify the opening statement and the three subsequent scenarios.
Notice how the author starts by reaffirming that the eternal cannot be a miracle, then immediately offers positive ways.
2
Understand the first way: direct creation
Focus on the first scenario: God creates the letters and words in the reciter's tongue without any power from the reciter. The miracle lies in the unique composition (nazm).
This preserves the miraculous nature by making the act entirely divine.
3
Explore the second and third ways
The second scenario involves God creating ordered speech in the prophet's soul, which the prophet then translates. The third scenario involves angelic mediation. Compare how each maintains the Quran's miraculous status.
In both, the prophet's tongue movement is within his power, but the speech content is divinely originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق أما السؤال الأول فنقول : القديم يستحيل أن يكون معجزة والمكتسب للعبد كذلك، وللقرآن وجوه من المعجزات ولكل وجه وجوه من التقديرات، فيجوز أن يقال : إن التلاوة من حيث إنها تلاوة معجزة وتقدير الإعجاز فيها من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يخلق الله تعالى هذه الحروف المركبة وتلك الكلمات المنظومة في لسان التالي من غير أن يكون قادرًا عليها ومحركا لسانه بقدرته واستطاعته، فعلا الله تعالى، ويظهر إعجازه في نظمه المخصوص، ويجوز أن يخلق الله في نفس النبي كلاما منظوما فيترجم عنه بلسانه، ويكون تحريك اللسان مقدوراً له لكن الكلام المعجز ما اشتمل عليه الضمير ونعت في الصدور، كما قال تعالى: (ولا تحرك به لسانك لتعجل به إن علينا جمعه وقرآنه) [القيامة: 16، 17] أي: في صدرك وقلبك، فإذا قرأناه أي: جمعناه فاتبع قرآنه [القيامة: 18]. ويجوز أن يخلق الله ذلك الكلام في قلب الملك أو في لسانه فيلقيه وحيًا إلى قلب النبي ويعبر عنه النبي بلسانه، كما قال تعالى: (إنه لقول رسول كريم) [التكوير: 19] أي: تنزيلاً ووحيًا، وكما قال: (علمه شديد القوى) [النجم: 5].
The people of truth said: As for the first objection, we say: The eternal is impossible to be a miracle, and likewise what is acquired by the servant. And the Quran has aspects of miracles, and for each aspect there are ways of estimation. So it is permissible to say: The recitation (tilawah) insofar as it is recitation is a miracle, and the estimation of inimitability in it is from two aspects: One of them: that God, exalted, creates these composite letters and those ordered words in the tongue of the reciter without him having power over them or moving his tongue by his own power and capacity—an act of God, exalted—and its inimitability appears in its particular composition. And it is permissible that God creates in the prophet's soul (nafs) ordered speech, and he translates it with his tongue, and the movement of the tongue is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the innermost self (damir) contained and described in the breasts, as God said: 'Move not your tongue with it to hasten it; indeed upon Us is its collection and its recitation' (75:16-17), meaning in your chest and heart; 'So when We have recited it, then follow its recitation' (75:18). And it is permissible that God creates that speech in the heart of the angel or on his tongue, and he reveals it as revelation to the heart of the prophet, and the prophet expresses it with his tongue, as God said: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger' (81:19), meaning as a sending down and revelation, and as He said: 'Taught by one severe in strength' (53:5).
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is the response (from Ahl al-Haqq) to the first objection. It begins by reaffirming that the eternal (qadim) cannot be a miracle and that what is acquired by the servant also cannot be a miracle. However, the Quran can be considered miraculous in multiple ways. The author presents two ways (wajhayn) to understand the inimitability of recitation (tilawah). The first way: God creates the letters and words directly in the reciter's tongue without any power or movement from the reciter, so the act is entirely God's, and the miracle lies in the specific composition (nazm) that appears. The second way: God creates ordered speech within the prophet's soul (nafs), and the prophet then translates it with his tongue. The movement of the tongue is within the prophet's power, but the miraculous speech is what the inner self (damir) contains, supported by Quranic verses (75:16-18). A third possibility is also mentioned: God creates the speech in the heart or tongue of the angel Jibril, who then reveals it to the prophet's heart, and the prophet expresses it with his tongue, citing verses 81:19 and 53:5. All these scenarios preserve the miraculous nature of the Quran while addressing the objections about its mode of existence.

Key Terms
قديم
معجزة
تلاوة
نظم
وحي
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the Quran is eternal, it cannot be a miracle in any sense.
Correction: The Quran's eternal essence is not a miracle, but its temporal manifestation (recitation, revelation) can be a miracle by divine action, as the passage explains.

Checkpoint
According to the response, which of the following is a way the Quran can be miraculous?
A) The prophet creates the Quran by his own power.
B) God creates the letters in the reciter's tongue without the reciter's power.
C) The angel writes the Quran on tablets.
D) The Quran is only miraculous in its meaning, not its words.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's eternal essence does not prevent it from being manifested as a miracle in time through divine action in recitation, prophetic inner speech, or angelic revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living as though each Quranic verse you hear is a direct, miraculous communication from God to you right now? What needs to change for that to become your reality?

Appearance of God's Eternal Speech in Letters and Sounds
States a second method: the manifestation of God's eternal speech through letters and sounds like Gabriel's manifestation in bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's eternal speech appears in temporal expressions without change, using the analogy of Gabriel's manifestation.

Core Idea
God's speech appears through letters without changing its eternal essence.

This lesson explores a second method for understanding the Qur'anic revelation: the eternal divine speech manifests through letters and sounds without undergoing change. The author uses the analogy of the angel Gabriel appearing in human form to illustrate this concept. By studying this passage, you will grasp a key argument for reconciling God's transcendence with the revealed scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, an analogy, and a logical deduction.
The passage is dense but rewards careful reading.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify how Gabriel's appearance in human form is used as an analogy. Gabriel's essence does not change, nor does he cease to be himself. Similarly, God's speech does not change when it appears in letters.
The analogy highlights manifestation without alteration.
3
Analyze the logical argument
The author rejects two alternatives: that God's speech changes into letters, or that it ceases and a new creation begins. Both are impossible for an eternal attribute.
Focus on the phrases 'قلب الأشخاص محال' and 'انعدمت حقيقة'.
4
Apply the concept
Reflect on how this argument defends the doctrine of the uncreated Qur'an. The speech is eternal, but its appearance in time does not imply change in God.
This is a standard Ash'ari position on the divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى طريقة أخرى ظهور كلام الباري تعالى بالعبارات والحروف والأصوات، وإن كان في نفسه واحدا أزليا كظهور جبريل بالأشخاص والأجسام والأعراض وإن كان في نفسه ذا حقيقة أخرى متقدما على الشخص، فإنه لا يقال : انقلبت حقيقته إلى حقيقة الجسمية في شخص معين؛ لأن قلب الأشخاص محال، وإن قيل : انعدمت حقيقة ووجدت حقيقة أخرى، فالثاني ليس بجبريل، فلا وجه إلا أن يقال : ظهر به ظهور المعنى بالعبارات، أو ظهور روح ما بشخص ما.
And on another method: the appearance of the speech of the Exalted Creator through expressions, letters, and sounds, even though it is in itself one and eternal, like the appearance of Gabriel through persons, bodies, and accidents, even though he is in himself of another reality preceding the person. For it is not said: his reality changed into the reality of corporeality in a specific person, because changing persons is impossible. And if it is said: one reality ceased and another reality came into existence, then the second is not Gabriel. So there is no alternative except to say: he appeared through it as the appearance of meaning through expressions, or the appearance of a spirit through a person.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative way to understand the Quranic revelation: the eternal divine speech appears through temporal letters and sounds without undergoing change. The analogy of Gabriel appearing in human forms is used: Gabriel's essence does not change into a body, but he manifests through bodies. Similarly, God's speech is one and eternal, yet manifests in letters. This guards against anthropomorphism and change in God.

Key Terms
كلام الباري
العبارات
الحروف
الأصوات
ظهور
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's speech changes into letters or becomes created.
Correction: God's speech is eternal and does not change; it appears in temporal expressions without alteration, just as Gabriel appears in human form without ceasing to be an angel.

Checkpoint

B) By being created new
C) By way of appearance without change
D) By ceasing to exist

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal and unchangeable; it appears in temporal letters without alteration, just as Gabriel appears in human form without ceasing to be an angel.

The Appearance of Eternal Speech in Expressions
The author compares the manifestation of divine speech to Jibril's appearance in human form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternal divine speech appears in temporal letters and sounds without undergoing change, using the analogy of Jibril's human form.

Core Idea
Divine speech appears in creation like Jibril in human form – essence unchanged, form new.

The author presents a profound analogy to resolve a theological puzzle: how can the uncreated, eternal speech of Allah be expressed in created, temporal letters and sounds? He compares this to the angel Jibril appearing in human form without his angelic essence being altered. This lesson unpacks that analogy, highlighting why transformation or replacement of essence is impossible, and how the Quran itself hints at this concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: the analogy, the rejected alternatives, the correct conclusion.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Identify the analogy's elements
Find how the author compares divine speech (كلام الباري) to Jibril (جبريل). What corresponds to letters and sounds? What corresponds to the human body?
Think: expressions are to speech as body is to Jibril.
3
Understand the rejected errors
The author rejects two wrong possibilities: that the essence changes into the form, or that the old essence ceases and a new one appears. Understand why each is impossible.
Change of persons is impossible; replacement would mean the second is not Jibril.
4
Grasp the correct conclusion
The only valid understanding is 'appearance' (ظهور): meaning appears through words, spirit appears through a person. This preserves the unity and eternity of divine speech.
The Quranic verse (al-An'am 6:9) supports this: if Allah sent an angel, He would make him appear as a man.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى طريقة أخرى ظهور كلام الباري تعالى بالعبارات والحروف والأصوات، وإن كان في نفسه واحدًا أزليا كظهور جبريل بالأشخاص والأجسام والأعراض وإن كان في نفسه ذا حقيقة أخرى متقدما على الشخص، فإنه لا يقال: انقلبت حقيقته إلى حقيقة الجسمية في شخص معين؛ لأن قلب الأشخاص محال، وإن قيل: انعدمت حقيقة ووجدت حقيقة أخرى، فالثاني ليس بجبريل، فلا وجه إلا أن يقال: ظهر به ظهور المعنى بالعبارات، أو ظهور روح ما بشخص ما. فكما صارت العبارات شخص المعنى، كذلك صارت صورة الأعرابي شخص الملك، وقد عبر القرآن عن مثل هذا المعنى: ( وَلَوْ جَعَلْتَهُ مَلَكًا لَّجَعَلْتَهُ رَجُلاً) [الأنعام: ۹] فكذلك يجب أن تفهم عبارات القرآن.
And according to another method: the appearance of the speech of the Exalted Creator in expressions, letters, and sounds, even though it is in itself one and eternal, like the appearance of Jibril in persons, bodies, and accidents even though he is in himself another reality preceding the person. For it is not said: his reality changed into the reality of corporeality in a specific person, because change of persons is impossible. And if it is said: a reality ceased and another reality came into existence, then the second is not Jibril. So there is no way except to say: he appeared through it as the appearance of meaning through expressions, or the appearance of a spirit through a person. Thus just as expressions become the personification of meaning, so the form of the bedouin became the personification of the angel. And the Quran expressed this meaning: 'And if We had made him an angel, We would have made him a man' [al-An'am 9]. So likewise must the expressions of the Quran be understood.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents another method for understanding how the eternal divine speech appears in temporal expressions without change. The author draws an analogy with the angel Jibril appearing in human form: Jibril's essence remains unchanged despite appearing in a body. Similarly, the eternal speech of God appears in letters and sounds without its essential unity being altered. The author rejects the idea of transformation or replacement, affirming that the appearance is like a meaning expressed through words. The Quranic verse (6:9) supports this: if God sent an angel as a messenger, He would make him appear as a man. Thus, the Quranic expressions should be understood as manifestations of the eternal speech.

Key Terms
كلام الباري
ظهور
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the eternal speech changes into temporal sounds, or that the original speech ceases and a new created speech replaces it.
Correction: The author clarifies that neither change nor replacement occurs; instead, the eternal speech appears through temporal expressions without any alteration to its essence, just as Jibril appears in human form without ceasing to be an angel.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to explain the appearance of divine speech in temporal expressions?
A) The imprint of a seal on wax
B) The appearance of an angel in human form
C) The reflection of the sun in water
D) The translation of a book into another language

Key Takeaway
Eternal divine speech appears in temporal expressions without change, just as Jibril appeared in human form without ceasing to be an angel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you feel you are encountering the eternal speech of Allah or just reading words? How would your recitation change if you truly internalized that the letters are manifestations of an uncreated reality?
?
The analogy with Jibril shows that a spiritual being can appear in a physical form without losing its reality. How does this reshape your understanding of the Quran's divine origin? Are there moments when you have treated the Quran as merely human words? What will you do to remind yourself of its eternal source?

The Two Types of Impossibility in the Naskh Debate
Classifies impossibility and frames the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between impossible in itself and impossible for leading to impossibility, and how this classification frames the Jewish objection to naskh.

Core Idea
Impossibility is not a single category; the objection hinges on naskh leading to an impossibility.

Before addressing the Jewish objection that abrogation implies badā' (change of mind) or regret in Allah, the author establishes a key distinction: impossibility is of two types. This passage explains that classification and then applies it to the case of naskh. Identifying which type of impossibility is at stake is crucial for the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of impossibility
Read the Arabic and translation to find how the author divides impossibility into two categories. Note the example given for the first type.
The example is the combination of black and white in one place.
2
Locate where naskh fits
The author says that lifting a duty after its establishment is impossible in itself. But the real issue is the second type: does naskh lead to impossibility?
3
Recognize the claimed impossibility
The author states that the only impossibility naskh could lead to is badā' and nadam (regret). This sets up the next lesson.
badā' and nadam are the terms that need further explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : المستحيل على نوعين؛ مستحيل لعينه كاجتماع السواد والبياض في محل واحد في حالة واحدة، ولا شك أن رفع التكليف وحكم التكليف بعد ثبوته من ذلك القبيل، ومستحيل لما يفضي إلى المحال كخلاف المعلوم، ولا محال هاهنا يفضي إليه النسخ إلا البدا والندم والبدا يطلق على معنيين؛ أحدهما الظهور، يقال : بدا له الشيء إذا ظهر ( وبدا لهم من الله ما لم يكونوا يحتسبون ) الزمر : ٤٧]، وهذا لا يجوز على الله تعالى، فإن المعلومات كلها ظاهرة لديه مكشوفة عنده، والنسخ لا يؤدي إلى ذلك؛ لأنه كان عالما بذلك التكليف عند توجهه على العبد، وكان عالما برفعه عند النسخ فلم يظهر له أمر متجدد لم يعلمه، ولا رفع الحكم لأنه ظهر له شيء آخر.
ويطلق البدا على الندم على ما كان والندم هو أن يقول قولا أو يفعل فعلا الغرض ثم يرى أن المصلحة في غير ما صدر عنه قولا وفعلا، وهذا أيضا لا يجوز في وصف الباري تعالى، فإنه لا يفعل فعلا لغرض، وإذا فعل بخلاف ذلك لم يفعله لمصلحة وغرض آخر، بل أقواله وأفعاله لا تعلل كما بينا قبل، فلم يتصور في حقه الندم، ولا أفضى النسخ إلى الندم، فتعين القول بأن النسخ غير مستحيل عقلا، وقوعه شرعًا من أدل الدليل على أنه غير مستحيل عقل.
It is said to them: The impossible is of two types: impossible in itself, like the combination of black and white in one locus at one state, and there is no doubt that raising the duty and the ruling of the duty after its establishment is of that category; and impossible for leading to impossibility, like contradicting known information. And here there is no impossibility to which naskh leads except badā' and nadam. And badā' is used in two senses: one is appearance (al-ẓuhūr). It is said: 'the thing appeared to him' if it became apparent. And {And there appeared to them from Allah what they had not reckoned} [az-Zumar 39:47]. And this is not permissible with respect to Allah Almighty, for all known things are apparent to Him and uncovered to Him. And naskh does not lead to that, because He was knowledgeable of that duty when it was directed upon the servant, and He was knowledgeable of its lifting at the time of naskh, so no new matter appeared to Him that He did not know, nor did He lift the ruling because something else appeared to Him. And badā' is also used for regret over what was, and regret is that one says a saying or does an action for a purpose, then sees that the benefit lies in something other than what proceeded from him in word and deed. And this too is not permissible in describing the Exalted Creator, for He does not do an action for a purpose, and if He does contrary to that, He does not do it for a benefit or another purpose. Rather, His words and actions are not caused by purposes as we have explained before. So regret is not conceivable with respect to Him, nor does naskh lead to regret. Thus it is established that naskh is not rationally impossible, and its occurrence according to the Shari'ah is the strongest evidence that it is not rationally impossible.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage refutes the Jewish objection by first classifying impossibility into two types: (1) impossible in itself (e.g., combining black and white), and (2) impossible for leading to an impossibility (e.g., contradicting known facts). The author notes that lifting a duty after its establishment is impossible in itself, but the issue here is whether naskh leads to an impossibility. The claimed impossibility is badā' and nadam. The author then analyzes badā', showing it has two meanings: (a) appearance (ẓuhūr) – something new becoming known. But this does not apply to Allah because He already knows all things. Naskh does not reveal anything new to Him; He knew the duty when He commanded it and knew its lifting when He abrogated it. (b) Regret (nadam) – which involves acting for a purpose and then realizing a better alternative. But Allah does not act for purposes or benefits; His actions are not caused by goals. Therefore, regret is impossible for Him. Since naskh leads to neither badā' in the sense of appearance nor nadam, it does not lead to an impossibility. Hence naskh is rationally possible, and its actual occurrence in revelation proves that it is not rationally impossible.

Key Terms
مستحيل
بداء
ندم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Jewish objection is based on naskh being impossible in itself.
Correction: The objection actually concerns the second type: naskh leading to impossibility (badā'/nadam). The author shows that naskh is not impossible in itself, but the real issue is whether it leads to something impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many types is impossibility divided?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Not specified

Key Takeaway
The classification of impossibility is the first step in refuting the Jewish objection: naskh must be examined not as impossible in itself, but as possibly leading to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself demanding that God’s actions fit into your limited categories of what is possible or impossible? Reflect on a time when you questioned a divine decree because it seemed contradictory. What does this classification teach you about trusting His wisdom, even when it appears to change?
?
The Jewish objection was based on accusing God of badā' (change of mind). Have you ever held a belief about God that, upon deeper reflection, implied an imperfection in Him? What does this lesson urge you to examine in your own understanding of God’s attributes?
?
In your life, do you sometimes insist that certain matters are impossible for God to change or abrogate? How does the distinction between 'impossible in itself' and 'impossible for leading to impossibility' challenge your assumptions about God’s actions?

The Two Meanings of Badā' and Why Naskh Does Not Imply Regret
Analyzes the two senses of badā' and concludes naskh is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of badā' (appearance or regret) and why neither applies to Allah, thus showing that naskh does not lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
Badā' does not necessarily imply ignorance or regret; properly understood, it poses no problem for divine abrogation.

Having shown that the only potential impossibility from naskh is badā' and nadam, the author now clarifies these two terms. Badā' can mean either 'the appearance of something new' or 'regret for a past action.' The passage demonstrates that neither meaning applies to Allah, and therefore naskh does not lead to impossibility. This completes the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first meaning of badā'
Read the explanation of badā' as 'appearance' (ẓuhūr). Note the Quranic verse cited. Then see why it does not apply to Allah: He already knows all things.
The verse is from az-Zumar 39:47.
2
Understand the second meaning of badā' as regret
Read how badā' is also used for nadam (regret). Regret involves acting for a purpose and then discovering a better alternative. The author explains why this is impossible for Allah: His actions are not for purposes or benefits.
The author references a previous discussion that actions of Allah are not caused by purposes.
3
Synthesize the conclusion
Since naskh leads to neither badā' (appearance) nor nadam, it does not lead to any impossibility. Therefore, naskh is rationally possible, and its actual occurrence in revelation confirms that.

Divine Attributes

Rulings Are Divine Speech, Not Inherent in Actions
The author states that rulings are divine speech, not inherent in actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that shar'i rulings like prohibition and obligation are not attributes of actions but are pure divine commands subject to change.

Core Idea
Rulings are speech of the Lawgiver, not qualities of actions.

The author begins by revealing the 'secret' behind the validity of abrogation: rulings are not intrinsic to actions. They are declared by the Lawgiver and can be altered. This first part clarifies the nature of rulings before establishing the broader principle of Allah's absolute discretion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the statement that rulings (hukm) are not attributes of actions (ṣifāt lahā) but refer back to the statements of the Lawgiver (aqwāl al-shāriʿ).
2
Notice the examples of abrogation
Observe the examples: prohibition of marriage lifted by contract, permissibility of wife lifted by divorce, differences between resident and traveler, men and women.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the rhetorical question: if rulings can change, what is impossible about changing laws between different peoples and times?
This sets up the argument against those who reject abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسر في ذلك كله أن الحظر والوجوب أحكام لا ترجع إلى الأفعال حتى تكون صفات لها، ولا الأفعال كانت على صفات من الحسن والقبح ورد الشرع بتقريرها، ولا قول الشارع أكسبها صفات لا تقبل الرفع والوضع، بل الأحكام راجعة إلى أقوال الشارع، وتوصف الأفعال بها قولا لا فعلا شرعا لا عقلا، فيجوز أن يرتفع بعضها ببعض، وذلك كالحرمة في الأجنبيات ترتفع بالعقد الصحيح، والحل في المنكوحة يرتفع بالطلاق المبين، وكأحكام المقيم تخالف أحكام المسافر، وأحكام الرجال في بعض الأحوال تخالف أحكام ربات الحجال، وإذا كانت الأحكام قابلة للرفع والوضع والتغيير والتبديل، فما المستحيل في وضع أحكام على أقوام في زمن ثم رفعها عن أقوام في زمن آخر، ثم يقابل التكليف بالأفعال، وتقابل التغييرات العقلية من الموت والحياة والصحة والسقم، والراحة والشدة، والنعمة والبلية، وإنبات الزرع وإفساده، وإنشاء الحيوان وإهلاكه، وخلق الإنسان وإفنائه تارة إلى مدة وتارة في الحال من غير مهلة تصريفه، لا بد أنهم حيث يشأ كما يشأ (لا يسأل عما يفعل) بالتغييرات التكليفية من الإباحة والحظر والإيجاب والإطلاق والمشي تارة إلى موضع والوقوف تارة في موضع، والقيام تارة والقعود أخرى تصريفا لعقولهم ونفوسهم، كما يشأ حيث يشأ (لا يسأل عما يفعل) فسبحان الذي بيده ملكوت كل شيء وإليه ترجعون. إليه أي له الملك والملك والخلق والأمر والفعل والقول والتصريف والتكليف، أليس الخلق الأول في مادة الإنسان يتصرف في تربيته من سلالة إلى نطفة إلى علقة إلى مضغة إلى عظام إلى كسوة العظام لحما إلى خلق آخر (فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين) أنشأ من طور إلى طور أبدا من كور إلى كورة، وكل مرتبة ناسخة للصورة الأولى لابسة صورة أخرى، ثم خالفت تلك الصورة إلى أخرى، كذلك الأمر الأول في كسوة صورة لنوع الإنسان يتصرف بتربيتها من شريعة إلى شريعة وكل شريعة ناسخة صورة ولابسة أخرى حتى ينتهي إلى كمال الشرائع كلها، وتختم بخاتم النبيين كلهم، وليس بعد الكمال والاستقامة إلا المعاد والقيامة بعثت أنا والساعة كهاتين فيتم الخلق هنالك، ويتم الأمر وتختم الخلقة بكمال حال النطفة إنسانا تاما، وتختم الشريعة بكمال حال الشرع الأول دينا تاما كاملا (اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم وأتممت عليكم نعمتي ورضيت لكم الإسلام دينا) رضينا بالله ربا وبالإسلام دينا وبمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم نبيا.
And the secret in all of that is that prohibition and obligation are rulings that do not refer back to actions such that they are attributes of them, nor were actions upon attributes of goodness and ugliness (ḥusn wa qubḥ) and the Law established them, nor did the Lawgiver's statement (qawl al-shāriʿ) acquire for them attributes that do not admit of elevation and lowering; rather, the rulings refer back to the statements of the Lawgiver, and actions are described by them in speech not in actuality, by way of law not reason. So it is possible that some of them are removed by others. That is like the prohibition [to marry] foreign women being removed by a valid contract, and the permissibility in the married woman being removed by a definitive divorce, and like the rulings of the resident differ from the rulings of the traveler, and the rulings of men in some situations differ from the rulings of women of the curtained chambers. And if the rulings are susceptible to elevation and lowering, alteration and change, then what is impossible in placing rulings on peoples in one time and then removing them from peoples in another time? Then [consider] the facing of obligation with actions, and the facing of intellectual changes of death and life, health and sickness, comfort and hardship, blessing and affliction, the growth of crops and their corruption, the creation of animals and their destruction, the creation of man and his annihilation, sometimes upon a duration and sometimes instantly without respite, His disposal, it is necessary that He does as He wills wherever He wills. (He is not asked about what He does) [Quran 21:23] with the prescriptive changes of permission and prohibition and obligation and release, walking sometimes to a place and standing sometimes in a place, standing sometimes and sitting another time, a disposal of their minds and souls, as He wills wherever He wills. (He is not asked about what He does) So glory be to Him in whose hand is the dominion of all things, and to Him you shall return. To Him, i.e., to Him belongs the sovereignty and the kingdom, creation and the command, action and speech, disposal and obligation. Is not the first creation in the matter of man—He disposes in his development from a drop (sulāla) to a clot (nuṭfa) to a clinging mass (ʿalaqa) to a chewed lump (muḍgha) to bones (ʿiẓām) to clothing the bones with flesh (kiswat al-ʿiẓām laḥman) to another creation (khalq ākhar)? (So blessed be Allah, the best of creators) [Quran 23:14] He brought forth from stage to stage forever, from one sphere (kawr) to another (kūra), and every station abrogates the previous form and puts on another form, then leaves that form for another. Likewise, the first command (al-amr al-awwal) in clothing a form for the human species disposes in its rearing from one law (sharīʿa) to another law, and every law abrogates a form and puts on another, until it reaches the perfection of all laws, and it is sealed with the seal of all prophets (khātam al-nabiyyīn). And after perfection and straightness there is nothing but the return (maʿād) and the resurrection (qiyāma). I and the Hour were sent like these two [fingers?], so creation is completed there, and the command is completed, and creation is sealed with the perfect state of the drop as a complete human, and the law is sealed with the perfect state of the first law as a complete and perfect religion. (Today I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you and chosen for you Islam as a religion) [Quran 5:3]. We are pleased with Allah as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad, peace be upon him, as prophet.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological argument defending the legitimacy of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law and drawing an analogy with Allah's creative control. The author begins by stating the 'secret' (sirr) behind the variability of rulings: prohibition (ḥaẓr) and obligation (wujūb) are not inherent attributes of actions; rather, they are pure divine commands declared by the Lawgiver (shāriʿ). Actions are described as permissible or forbidden only by speech (qawl) of the Law, not by actual property (fiʿl) or reason (ʿaql). Therefore, rulings can be elevated or lowered, changed or replaced by other rulings. The author gives examples: the prohibition of marriage with certain foreign women (ajnabiyyāt) is removed by a valid marriage contract; the permissibility (ḥill) of a wife is removed by a pronounced divorce; different rules apply to residents vs. travelers, and men vs. women. Since rulings are mutable, there is no impossibility in Allah instituting one set of laws for a people at one time and later superseding them with another set. The author then broadens the argument to Allah's absolute sovereignty: just as Allah controls all natural changes (death, life, health, etc.) and is not questioned (lā yusʾalu ʿammā yafʿal), so too He controls prescriptive legal changes (permission, prohibition, obligation) according to His will. The passage climaxes with a doxology: 'So glory be to Him in whose hand is the dominion of all things, and to Him you shall return.' (Quran 36:83). The second half (page 503) begins by clarifying that to Him belongs all sovereignty, creation, command, action, speech, disposal (taṣrīf), and obligation (taklīf). Then an analogy is drawn from the physical creation of man: Allah develops the human embryo in stages (from nutfa to ʿalaqa to muḍgha to bones to flesh to another creation), each stage abrogating (nāsikha) the previous form and 'putting on' (lābisa) a new one. Similarly, the divine command (al-amr al-awwal) in shaping the human species proceeds through successive sharīʿas, each abrogating the previous until it reaches perfection in the seal of the prophets and the completion of all laws. After perfection, there is only the return and resurrection. The author quotes a hadith: 'I and the Hour were sent like these two [fingers?],' indicating the closeness of the end. Thus, creation and law are both completed: creation with the perfect human form, and law with the perfect religion (Islam), citing Quran 5:3. The passage ends with a declaration of acceptance: 'We are pleased with Allah as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet.'

Key Terms
حظر (prohibition)
وجوب (obligation)
أحكام (rulings)
قول الشارع (statement of the Lawgiver)
نسخ (abrogation)
حسن وقبح (goodness and ugliness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rulings (like prohibition and obligation) are intrinsic properties of actions and cannot change.
Correction: The passage clarifies that rulings are not attributes of actions; they are statements of the Lawgiver and can be elevated or lowered, changed or replaced.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for rulings like prohibition and obligation?
A) They are inherent attributes of the actions themselves.
B) They are purely based on the statements of the Lawgiver (shāriʿ).
C) They are determined by human reason and moral intuition.
D) They are immutable and cannot change throughout history.

Key Takeaway
Rulings are mutable divine commands, not fixed qualities of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that an action is 'obviously' good or evil without consulting Allah's command? How does this lesson challenge your natural moral instinct to rely on your own judgment? Will you actively seek the ruling from Allah's speech before deciding?
?
The author argues that rulings can change because they are not attached to actions but to Allah's will. Does this flexibility comfort you, making you feel that Allah's law is merciful and adaptable, or does it unsettle you because it implies that what was once commanded may no longer apply? Are you willing to trust His wisdom even when it means some rules are for others and not for you?
?
Consider a ruling from the Quran or Sunnah that you struggle to understand or accept. If you truly believe it is Allah's speech, then your struggle is not with the ruling but with your own desires. What is your heart's response to that ruling right now—submission, rationalization, or rebellion? What concrete step will you take today to align your heart with the Lawgiver?

Allah's Absolute Control Over Natural and Legal Changes
The author draws an analogy between Allah's control over natural changes and His control over legal rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that just as Allah controls all natural changes without being questioned, He also controls prescriptive legal changes according to His will.

Core Idea
Allah's disposal of creation and law is absolute and unquestionable.

Having established that rulings can change, the author now expands the argument to Allah's absolute sovereignty. He parallels natural changes (life, death, health) with legal changes (permission, prohibition) and emphasizes that Allah is not asked about what He does.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage noting the parallel
Read the Arabic and translation, identifying the two sets: natural changes and legal changes. Notice the structure: 'Then [consider] the facing of obligation with actions, and the facing of intellectual changes...'
2
Identify the repeated phrase
Find the phrase 'He is not asked about what He does' (لا يسأل عما يفعل). It appears twice. Reflect on its significance for both natural and legal domains.
3
Reflect on the doxology
The passage ends with a glorification: 'So glory be to Him in whose hand is the dominion of all things, and to Him you shall return.' Consider how this reinforces the message.
This doxology is a quote or allusion to Quran 36:83.
4
Connect to the previous lesson
Understand that this lesson grounds the mutability of rulings in the broader principle of Allah's absolute will and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسر في ذلك كله أن الحظر والوجوب أحكام لا ترجع إلى الأفعال حتى تكون صفات لها، ولا الأفعال كانت على صفات من الحسن والقبح ورد الشرع بتقريرها، ولا قول الشارع أكسبها صفات لا تقبل الرفع والوضع، بل الأحكام راجعة إلى أقوال الشارع، وتوصف الأفعال بها قولا لا فعلا شرعا لا عقلا، فيجوز أن يرتفع بعضها ببعض، وذلك كالحرمة في الأجنبيات ترتفع بالعقد الصحيح، والحل في المنكوحة يرتفع بالطلاق المبين، وكأحكام المقيم تخالف أحكام المسافر، وأحكام الرجال في بعض الأحوال تخالف أحكام ربات الحجال، وإذا كانت الأحكام قابلة للرفع والوضع والتغيير والتبديل، فما المستحيل في وضع أحكام على أقوام في زمن ثم رفعها عن أقوام في زمن آخر، ثم يقابل التكليف بالأفعال، وتقابل التغييرات العقلية من الموت والحياة والصحة والسقم، والراحة والشدة، والنعمة والبلية، وإنبات الزرع وإفساده، وإنشاء الحيوان وإهلاكه، وخلق الإنسان وإفنائه تارة إلى مدة وتارة في الحال من غير مهلة تصريفه، لا بد أنهم حيث يشأ كما يشأ (لا يسأل عما يفعل) بالتغييرات التكليفية من الإباحة والحظر والإيجاب والإطلاق والمشي تارة إلى موضع والوقوف تارة في موضع، والقيام تارة والقعود أخرى تصريفا لعقولهم ونفوسهم، كما يشأ حيث يشأ (لا يسأل عما يفعل) فسبحان الذي بيده ملكوت كل شيء وإليه ترجعون. إليه أي له الملك والملك والخلق والأمر والفعل والقول والتصريف والتكليف، أليس الخلق الأول في مادة الإنسان يتصرف في تربيته من سلالة إلى نطفة إلى علقة إلى مضغة إلى عظام إلى كسوة العظام لحما إلى خلق آخر (فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين) أنشأ من طور إلى طور أبدا من كور إلى كورة، وكل مرتبة ناسخة للصورة الأولى لابسة صورة أخرى، ثم خالفت تلك الصورة إلى أخرى، كذلك الأمر الأول في كسوة صورة لنوع الإنسان يتصرف بتربيتها من شريعة إلى شريعة وكل شريعة ناسخة صورة ولابسة أخرى حتى ينتهي إلى كمال الشرائع كلها، وتختم بخاتم النبيين كلهم، وليس بعد الكمال والاستقامة إلا المعاد والقيامة بعثت أنا والساعة كهاتين فيتم الخلق هنالك، ويتم الأمر وتختم الخلقة بكمال حال النطفة إنسانا تاما، وتختم الشريعة بكمال حال الشرع الأول دينا تاما كاملا (اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم وأتممت عليكم نعمتي ورضيت لكم الإسلام دينا) رضينا بالله ربا وبالإسلام دينا وبمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم نبيا.
And the secret in all of that is that prohibition and obligation are rulings that do not refer back to actions such that they are attributes of them, nor were actions upon attributes of goodness and ugliness (ḥusn wa qubḥ) and the Law established them, nor did the Lawgiver's statement (qawl al-shāriʿ) acquire for them attributes that do not admit of elevation and lowering; rather, the rulings refer back to the statements of the Lawgiver, and actions are described by them in speech not in actuality, by way of law not reason. So it is possible that some of them are removed by others. That is like the prohibition [to marry] foreign women being removed by a valid contract, and the permissibility in the married woman being removed by a definitive divorce, and like the rulings of the resident differ from the rulings of the traveler, and the rulings of men in some situations differ from the rulings of women of the curtained chambers. And if the rulings are susceptible to elevation and lowering, alteration and change, then what is impossible in placing rulings on peoples in one time and then removing them from peoples in another time? Then [consider] the facing of obligation with actions, and the facing of intellectual changes of death and life, health and sickness, comfort and hardship, blessing and affliction, the growth of crops and their corruption, the creation of animals and their destruction, the creation of man and his annihilation, sometimes upon a duration and sometimes instantly without respite, His disposal, it is necessary that He does as He wills wherever He wills. (He is not asked about what He does) [Quran 21:23] with the prescriptive changes of permission and prohibition and obligation and release, walking sometimes to a place and standing sometimes in a place, standing sometimes and sitting another time, a disposal of their minds and souls, as He wills wherever He wills. (He is not asked about what He does) So glory be to Him in whose hand is the dominion of all things, and to Him you shall return. To Him, i.e., to Him belongs the sovereignty and the kingdom, creation and the command, action and speech, disposal and obligation. Is not the first creation in the matter of man—He disposes in his development from a drop (sulāla) to a clot (nuṭfa) to a clinging mass (ʿalaqa) to a chewed lump (muḍgha) to bones (ʿiẓām) to clothing the bones with flesh (kiswat al-ʿiẓām laḥman) to another creation (khalq ākhar)? (So blessed be Allah, the best of creators) [Quran 23:14] He brought forth from stage to stage forever, from one sphere (kawr) to another (kūra), and every station abrogates the previous form and puts on another form, then leaves that form for another. Likewise, the first command (al-amr al-awwal) in clothing a form for the human species disposes in its rearing from one law (sharīʿa) to another law, and every law abrogates a form and puts on another, until it reaches the perfection of all laws, and it is sealed with the seal of all prophets (khātam al-nabiyyīn). And after perfection and straightness there is nothing but the return (maʿād) and the resurrection (qiyāma). I and the Hour were sent like these two [fingers?], so creation is completed there, and the command is completed, and creation is sealed with the perfect state of the drop as a complete human, and the law is sealed with the perfect state of the first law as a complete and perfect religion. (Today I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you and chosen for you Islam as a religion) [Quran 5:3]. We are pleased with Allah as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad, peace be upon him, as prophet.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological argument defending the legitimacy of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law and drawing an analogy with Allah's creative control. The author begins by stating the 'secret' (sirr) behind the variability of rulings: prohibition (ḥaẓr) and obligation (wujūb) are not inherent attributes of actions; rather, they are pure divine commands declared by the Lawgiver (shāriʿ). Actions are described as permissible or forbidden only by speech (qawl) of the Law, not by actual property (fiʿl) or reason (ʿaql). Therefore, rulings can be elevated or lowered, changed or replaced by other rulings. The author gives examples: the prohibition of marriage with certain foreign women (ajnabiyyāt) is removed by a valid marriage contract; the permissibility (ḥill) of a wife is removed by a pronounced divorce; different rules apply to residents vs. travelers, and men vs. women. Since rulings are mutable, there is no impossibility in Allah instituting one set of laws for a people at one time and later superseding them with another set. The author then broadens the argument to Allah's absolute sovereignty: just as Allah controls all natural changes (death, life, health, etc.) and is not questioned (lā yusʾalu ʿammā yafʿal), so too He controls prescriptive legal changes (permission, prohibition, obligation) according to His will. The passage climaxes with a doxology: 'So glory be to Him in whose hand is the dominion of all things, and to Him you shall return.' (Quran 36:83). The second half (page 503) begins by clarifying that to Him belongs all sovereignty, creation, command, action, speech, disposal (taṣrīf), and obligation (taklīf). Then an analogy is drawn from the physical creation of man: Allah develops the human embryo in stages (from nutfa to ʿalaqa to muḍgha to bones to flesh to another creation), each stage abrogating (nāsikha) the previous form and 'putting on' (lābisa) a new one. Similarly, the divine command (al-amr al-awwal) in shaping the human species proceeds through successive sharīʿas, each abrogating the previous until it reaches perfection in the seal of the prophets and the completion of all laws. After perfection, there is only the return and resurrection. The author quotes a hadith: 'I and the Hour were sent like these two [fingers?],' indicating the closeness of the end. Thus, creation and law are both completed: creation with the perfect human form, and law with the perfect religion (Islam), citing Quran 5:3. The passage ends with a declaration of acceptance: 'We are pleased with Allah as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet.'

Key Terms
إباحة (permission)
حظر (prohibition)
وجوب (obligation)
إطلاق (release)
تكليف (obligation)
ملكوت (dominion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What phrase is repeated in the passage to emphasize Allah's absolute authority over both natural and legal changes?
A) 'To Him you shall return'
B) 'He is not asked about what He does'
C) 'The dominion of all things'
D) 'As He wills wherever He wills'

Key Takeaway
Allah is not questioned about His disposal of both creation and law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Parallel Between Human Creation and Successive Laws
The author analogizes the stages of human creation to the succession of divine laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the succession of divine laws is analogous to the stages of human embryonic development, both leading to perfection in Islam.

Core Idea
Just as the embryo develops through abrogated stages, so do the laws culminate in Islam.

In the final part of this passage, the author draws a powerful analogy: the creation of man in stages (from drop to perfect form) mirrors the progression of divine laws from earlier shari'as to the final, perfect Islam. This illustrates the wisdom of abrogation and the sealing of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of human development
Read the Arabic passage describing the stages of human creation: from a drop (sulāla) to a complete human. Notice the verbs: 'disposes' (yataṣarraf), 'abrogates' (nāsikha), 'puts on' (lābisa).
2
Trace the parallel in laws
Identify how the author explicitly draws the analogy: 'Likewise, the first command in clothing a form for the human species disposes in its rearing from one law to another law.'
3
Understand the culmination
Both processes end in perfection: creation reaches a perfect human (insān tāmm), and law reaches the perfect religion (dīn tāmm kāmil). The seal of the prophets (khātam al-nabiyyīn) marks the end.
The hadith about the Hour emphasizes the imminence of the end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the perfection of Islam means that after it there is only the resurrection and the return to Allah, does your daily life reflect that urgency and focus? Or do you live as if there is still time for something else to come? What would you need to change to truly live in the shadow of the Hour?
?
The passage ends with 'We are pleased with Allah as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet.' Are you genuinely content with this, or is there a hidden dissatisfaction that makes you wish for alternatives? Examine your heart: do you truly embrace the finality of Islam's message, or do you long for a new 'stage' that fits your desires?
